Divine Decree
beginner
4 min
Human Action and Acquisition (Kasb)
States the Ash'ari doctrine on human action and kasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that human actions are created by God's power and that humans only acquire (kasb) those actions, earning responsibility without being the creator.

Core Idea
God creates all actions; humans only acquire them.

This lesson introduces the first section of the fifth chapter on human actions. The Ash'ari position is that while God is the sole creator of all actions, humans are not mere puppets — they 'acquire' their actions, which is the basis for moral responsibility. Let's explore this foundational doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: 'فعل العبد', 'بقدرة الله', 'الكسب'.
2
Understand the key concept 'kasb'
The term 'الكسب' means 'acquisition'. It is the Ash'ari mechanism for human agency: humans do not create their actions but they 'acquire' them, meaning they intend and perform the action by God's power. This distinguishes Ash'aris from Qadaris and Jabaris.
Kasb is a technical term in Ash'ari theology.
3
Contrast with other positions
Consider how this differs from the Mu'tazilite view (humans create their own actions) and the Jabari view (no human agency). The Ash'ari middle path affirms both God's omnipotence and human responsibility.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this doctrine matters for ethics and divine justice. How can God reward or punish for actions He creates? The answer lies in kasb: humans earn the action through their intention and effort.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الفصل الخامس في الأفعال وفيه أربعة مباحث : الأول : فعل العبد واقع بقدرة الله تعالى، وإنما للعبد الكسب.
Then the fifth chapter on actions, and it contains four sections. The first: the action of the servant is realized by the power of God Most High, and the servant only has acquisition (kasb).
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth chapter on actions (الأفعال), which contains four sections. It then states the first section's doctrine: the action of the servant (فعل العبد) is realized (واقع) by the power of God Most High (بقدرة الله تعالى), and the servant only has acquisition (الكسب). This is the classic Ash'ari position on human agency: God is the true creator of all actions, while humans acquire (يكتسب) the action, earning responsibility.

Key Terms
فعل العبد
قدرة الله
الكسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans create their own actions independently of God.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that actions occur by God's power, and humans only have kasb (acquisition). So humans do not create actions; they acquire them from God.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the role of the servant in his actions?
A) He creates them independently.
B) He only has acquisition (kasb).
C) He has no role at all.
D) He shares power with God.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari doctrine holds that all actions are created by God's power, and humans only acquire (kasb) them, which grounds moral responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent good deed you did. Did you feel a sense of pride as if you were the ultimate cause? How does the teaching that God creates all actions and you only acquire them confront that pride? What will you do the next time you succeed in a good deed to keep your heart humble?
?
Consider a sin you struggle with. Does knowing that the sin itself occurs by God's power weaken your sense of responsibility? According to the concept of kasb, why should it not? What change in your repentance practice does this require to truly take ownership of your sins?

Mu'tazila on Evil and Human Acts: A Comparative View
Mu'tazila on evil attribution and human acts, compared to dualists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's attribution of evil to Shaytan and their view on human acts, and why the author compares them to dualists.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's attribution of evil to Shaytan mirrors dualist theology, while human free will creates a tension.

This passage situates the Mu'tazila within Islamic theological debates. It highlights their unique position on the source of evil and the creation of human acts, drawing a parallel with dualist religions. This lesson unpacks that comparison and sets the stage for the later discussion on khalq al-a'mal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the two claims: evil attributed to Shaytan, voluntary acts attributed to humans and animals.
The author uses very concise language.
2
Identify the comparison
The author says the Mu'tazila view is close to the dualist doctrine of light and darkness. Ask: Why is that comparison important?
Dualists posit two eternal principles of good and evil.
3
Recognize the theological issue
The attribution of human acts is flagged as the problem of khalq al-a'mal. Understand that this signals a major debate about free will and divine omnipotence.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider how this position attempts to distance God from evil while preserving human responsibility.
Compare to other Islamic sects if familiar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما من المسلمين. فالمعتزلة أسندوا الشرور والقبائح إلى الشيطان وهو قريب من مذهب القائلين بالنور والظلمة، وأسندوا الأفعال الاختيارية للإنسان وغيره من الحيوانات البهم، وهو مسألة خلق الأعمال وستأتي.
And as for the Muslims: the Mu'tazila attributed evils and ugly acts to Shaytan, which is close to the doctrine of the proponents of light and darkness. And they attributed voluntary actions to humans and other dumb animals, and this is the issue of the creation of acts (khalq al-a'mal), which will come later.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila position on two issues. First, they attribute evil and morally ugly actions to Shaytan rather than directly to God. The author notes that this is close to the dualist doctrine of those who posit two eternal principles of light and darkness (good and evil). Second, they attribute voluntary acts (al-af'al al-ikhtiyariyya) to humans and even to non-rational animals, meaning that these beings are the actual creators of their own acts. The author identifies this as the well-known theological problem of 'created acts' (khalq al-a'mal) and indicates that it will be discussed in detail later.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الشرور والقبائح
الشيطان
القائلين بالنور والظلمة
خلق الأعمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila completely deny divine causality.
Correction: Their attribution of evil to Shaytan is a form of modified dualism, not a full denial of God's role; God remains the creator of good acts and essences.

Checkpoint
Which group does the author compare the Mu'tazila to because of their attribution of evil to Shaytan?
A) The philosophers.
B) The astrologers.
C) The proponents of light and darkness.
D) The Sabians.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's attribution of evil to Shaytan places them close to dualism, while their attribution of human acts as created by humans raises the foundational issue of khalq al-a'mal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or evil in your life, do you find yourself blaming others (like Shaytan) or questioning God's justice? How does this lesson's comparison with the Mu'tazila challenge your own assumptions about the source of evil?
?
The Mu'tazila tried to protect God's justice by shifting evil to Shaytan. In your own heart, do you ever secretly feel that God could have prevented some evil? What does this lesson reveal about your own internal theodicy?
?
If you truly believe that all acts are created by God (as Sunni doctrine holds), how does that change the way you take responsibility for your own voluntary actions? Do you sometimes excuse yourself by blaming circumstances or others, like the Mu'tazila blamed Shaytan?

Divine Decree

4 min
Four Key Topics on Divine Actions
Chapter heading enumerating four research topics on divine actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four research topics that define the Ash‘arī approach to divine actions.

Core Idea
Divine actions are understood through four Ash‘arī principles: acquisition, universal will, moral neutrality of reason, and God's transcendence of evil.

This lesson introduces the foundational chapter on divine actions. The chapter presents four research topics (mabāḥith) that together outline the Ash‘arī theological framework regarding human actions, divine will, and moral epistemology. Each topic addresses a central question about the relationship between God and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter heading and translation
Read the full Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that it lists four separate topics under the heading 'On Actions'.
2
Understand the doctrine of acquisition (kasb)
The first topic states that a servant’s action occurs by God’s power, and the servant only has ‘acquisition’. This is the Ash‘arī solution to the problem of free will and determinism.
3
Grasp the universality of God's will
The second topic asserts that God’s will encompasses everything. Nothing happens outside His will, affirming divine sovereignty.
4
Analyze the rejection of rational moral judgment
The third topic denies that reason can independently determine good and evil in terms of moral desert. This challenges rationalist ethics.
5
Consider the statement that nothing ugly comes from God
The fourth topic declares that nothing morally evil (qabīḥ) can be attributed to God, emphasizing His absolute perfection and justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس
وفيه مباحث :
في الأفعال
الأول : فعل العبد واقع بقدرة الله تعالى
وإنما للعبد الكسب .
الثاني : في عموم إرادته .
الثالث : لا حكم للعقل بالحسن والقبح بمعنى استحقاق المدح والذم .
الرابع: لا قبيح من الله تعالى.
Chapter Five, containing research topics: On actions. First: The action of the servant occurs by the power of God Most High, and the servant only has acquisition (al-kasb). Second: On the universality of His will. Third: There is no judgment of reason concerning good and evil in the sense of deserving praise and blame. Fourth: There is nothing ugly (evil) from God Most High.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading for Chapter Five, listing four research topics (mabāḥith) under the general subject of divine actions (al-afʿāl). The first topic affirms the Ashʿarī doctrine of kasb: human actions are truly created by God's power, and humans merely 'acquire' them. The second topic asserts the universality of God's will, meaning nothing occurs outside His will. The third topic denies that reason can independently judge actions as good or evil in terms of moral desert (istiḥqāq al-madḥ wa al-dhamm); this is a foundational Ashʿarī rejection of rationalist ethics. The fourth topic declares that nothing from God can be considered morally evil (qabīḥ), affirming God's absolute sovereignty and justice beyond human categories.

Key Terms
فعل العبد
قدرة الله
الكسب
عموم الإرادة
الحسن والقبح
استحقاق المدح والذم
لا قبيح من الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The doctrine of kasb means humans create their own actions and God simply approves.
Correction: In Ashʿarism, kasb means God creates the act and the human 'acquires' it, meaning the human is the locus of the act and thus responsible, but God is the sole creator.

Checkpoint
What does the third research topic of Chapter Five address?
A) Human free will
B) Reason's judgment of good and evil as deserving praise or blame
C) God's universal will
D) The nature of divine power

Key Takeaway
The four topics of Chapter Five outline the Ashʿarī framework for understanding divine actions, emphasizing God's exclusive creative power, universal will, and the limitations of human reason in moral evaluation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a choice, do you truly feel that every detail – even your intention – is brought into being by God? How would your daily decisions change if you lived with the certainty of al-kasb?
?
Have you ever secretly thought that something in your life was too evil or unjust to come from God? How does the fourth topic ('لا قبيح من الله') confront that thought, and what would it mean for you to surrender your sense of grievance?
?
If reason cannot determine what deserves praise or blame, how do you know that your sense of outrage at a personal wrong is valid? Are you willing to let divine revelation alone define your moral judgments, even when they clash with your emotions?

Divergent Views on Human Action
Enumeration of sectarian views on human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different theological positions on the power behind human actions, from the Ashʿarī view of acquisition to the Muʿtazilī view of human power, and why pure determinism is rejected.

Core Idea
Human action sparks a spectrum of opinions: from divine power alone to human power alone.

This passage opens the fifth chapter on divine actions by immediately diving into the first investigation: the human act. The author states the Ashʿarī position that the act occurs by God's power and the human has only acquisition, then contrasts it with the views of the Muʿtazila, philosophers, al-Ustādh, and al-Qāḍī. He also dismisses pure determinism (jabr) as necessarily false. Let's explore these positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ashʿarī position
Read the opening sentence: 'فعل العبد واقع بقدرة الله تعالى، وإنما للعبد الكسب'. Notice that the act is attributed to God's power and the human only 'acquires' it.
2
Contrast with other sects
The passage lists four other views: Muʿtazila (human power), philosophers (necessitation), al-Ustādh (both powers together), al-Qāḍī (God's power on essence, human power on quality). How do they differ?
Al-Ustādh is Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī; al-Qāḍī is al-Bāqillānī.
3
Understand the rejection of jabr
The author states that pure compulsion (jabr) is 'necessarily false' because it denies any effect of human power. Reflect on why a middle ground (kasb) is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمعنى
الفصل الخامس
في أفعاله
قال الفصل الخامس في أفعاله وفيه مباحث (۱) .
المبحث الأول : فعل العبد واقع بقدرة الله تعالى، وإنما للعبد الكسب . والمعتزلة : بقدرة العبد صحة، والحكماء إيجاباً، والأستاذ بهما على أن يتعلقا جميعاً به، والقاضي على أن تتعلق قدرة الله ،بأصله وقدرة العبد بوضعه ككونه طاعة أو معصية، وأما الجبر أنه لا أثر لقدرة العبد أصلاً لا إيجاداً ولا كسباً فضروري البطلان، والكسب قبل ذلك الوصف الذي به تتعلق قدرة العبد وقيل الفعل المخلوق بقدرة الله من حيث خلق للعبد قدرة متعلقة به وقيل ما يقع به المقدور بلا صحة انفراد القادر. وما يقع في محل القدرة، والحق أنه ظاهر والخفاء في التعبير، والأوضح أنه أمر إضافي يجب من العبد، ولا يوجب وجود المقدور، بل اتصاف الفاعل بالمقدور كتعيين أحد الطرفين ،وترجيحه، وصرف القدرة .
(۱) راجع ما كتبه ابن حزم في هذا الموضوع فهو في غاية الوجاهة ورده على عباد بن سليمان تلميذ هشام بن عمر و الغوطي الذي قال: إن الله تعالى لم يخلق الكفار لأنهم ناس وكفر معاً لكن خلق
أجسامهم دون كفرهم جـ ٣ ص ٥٤ وما بعدها .
By meaning. The fifth chapter: On His actions. He said: The fifth chapter on His actions, and it contains investigations (1). The first investigation: The act of the slave occurs by the power of God most high, and the slave has only acquisition (kasb). And the Mu'tazila: by the power of the slave it is valid, and the philosophers: necessitation, and al-Ustadh: both of them together attached to it, and al-Qadi: that the power of God attaches to its origin and the power of the slave to its description like its being obedience or disobedience. As for jabr (compulsion), that there is no effect for the slave's power at all, neither creation nor acquisition, it is necessarily false. And al-kasb before that: the description by which the power of the slave attaches, and it is said: the act created by God's power insofar as He created for the slave a power related to it, and it is said: that by which the intended object occurs without the independent validity of the agent. And what occurs in the locus of power. And the truth is that it is apparent, and the obscurity is in the expression. And the clearest is that it is a relational matter necessitated from the slave, and does not cause the existence of the intended object, but rather the description of the agent by the intended object, like determining one of the two alternatives, and giving it preponderance, and directing the power. (1) Refer to what Ibn Hazm wrote on this subject, for it is extremely sound, and his refutation of 'Abbad ibn Sulayman, the student of Hisham ibn 'Amr al-Ghuti, who said that God most high did not create the disbelievers because they are people and disbelief together, but He created their bodies without their disbelief, vol. 3, p. 54 and after.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage opens the fifth chapter on God's actions and immediately addresses the first investigation (mabḥath): the human act. The author states the Ashʿarī position: the human act occurs by God's power, and the human has only acquisition (kasb). He then contrasts this with other views: the Muʿtazila affirm that the act is valid by the human's own power; the philosophers (ḥukamāʾ) hold that it is necessitated (ījāb); al-Ustādh (Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī) says both powers attach together; al-Qāḍī (al-Bāqillānī) distinguishes God's power attaching to the act's essence and human power attaching to its quality (e.g., obedience or disobedience). The author then dismisses pure determinism (jabr) as necessarily false—since it denies any effect of human power whatsoever, whether in creation or acquisition. Next, he defines kasb (acquisition) through three interpretations: (1) the description by which human power attaches to the act; (2) the act created by God's power insofar as He creates for the human a power related to it; (3) that by which the intended object (maqdūr) occurs without the independent validity of the agent—i.e., the human does not create independently. He notes that the concept is intuitively clear, but the difficulty lies in its verbal expression. The clearest explanation, he says, is that kasb is a relational matter (amr iḍāfī) that is necessitated by the human but does not cause the existence of the act; rather, it is the attribution of the act to the human agent—like specifying one of two alternatives, giving it preponderance, and directing the power. The footnote directs the reader to Ibn Ḥazm's refutation of ʿAbbād ibn Sulaymān, who denied that God creates the disbelief of disbelievers.

Key Terms
فعل العبد
قدرة الله
الكسب
الجبر
المعتزلة
الحكماء
الأستاذ
القاضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ashʿarī kasb means humans create their own acts.
Correction: The passage clearly states the act occurs by God's power; kasb is not creation but a relational attribution.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the Mu'tazila believe about human action?
A) Human action occurs solely by God's power.
B) Human action occurs by the human's own power.
C) Human action is a combination of divine and human power.
D) Human action is an illusion with no real effect.

Key Takeaway
The Ashʿarī position distinguishes itself from other sects by affirming God's exclusive creative power while still giving the human a role through acquisition (kasb).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage describes the Ashʿarī view: your every act occurs by God's power, and you only 'acquire' it. In which areas of your life do you most resist this truth? Think of a habit you struggle with—do you pray as if God alone enables change, or do you secretly trust your own power? What would a real, daily reliance on God's power over your actions look like today?
?
The Muʿtazila claim that your own power suffices to produce your actions. How often do you unconsciously act as a Muʿtazilī, feeling self-sufficient in your obedience or despairing in your failure as if God is not involved? Identify one specific action you will perform today—a prayer, a kind word, or a task—and consciously affirm before doing it: 'This act occurs only by God's power, and I acquire it through His will.' Will you do that?
?
When you sin, do you ever blame God or feel that He compelled you? The passage rejects jabr as 'necessarily false.' In what specific situation have you subtly excused your sin by blaming circumstance or divine decree? Make a firm resolve to confess that sin directly to God, taking full ownership (kasb) of your choice, and ask Him to forgive the act He created and you acquired.

Defining Acquisition (Kasb)
Definition and clarification of the term kasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main definitions of kasb (acquisition) and the author’s preferred clarification that it is a relational matter attributed to the human without causing the act’s existence.

Core Idea
Kasb is the human's relational contribution without causing existence.

Having presented the competing views on human power, the author now defines the key Ashʿarī concept of kasb (acquisition). He offers three definitions and then gives his own clarification, emphasizing that kasb is a relational matter (amr iḍāfī) that does not bring the act into existence but rather attributes it to the human agent. Let's unpack these definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of kasb
The passage gives three definitions: (1) the description by which human power attaches, (2) the act created by God insofar as He creates a power for the human, (3) the means by which the intended object occurs without the agent's independent validity. Compare them.
2
Understand the author's preferred view
The author says the truth is apparent but expression is obscure. He clarifies that kasb is a relational matter: it is necessitated by the human but does not cause the act's existence. Instead, it is the description of the agent by the act—like choosing one alternative over another.
This avoids both jabr (no human role) and Muʿtazilī independence.
3
Reflect on why this matters
Kasb preserves divine omnipotence while allowing human moral responsibility. How does this definition achieve that balance?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمعنى
الفصل الخامس
في أفعاله
قال الفصل الخامس في أفعاله وفيه مباحث (۱) .
المبحث الأول : فعل العبد واقع بقدرة الله تعالى، وإنما للعبد الكسب . والمعتزلة : بقدرة العبد صحة، والحكماء إيجاباً، والأستاذ بهما على أن يتعلقا جميعاً به، والقاضي على أن تتعلق قدرة الله ،بأصله وقدرة العبد بوضعه ككونه طاعة أو معصية، وأما الجبر أنه لا أثر لقدرة العبد أصلاً لا إيجاداً ولا كسباً فضروري البطلان، والكسب قبل ذلك الوصف الذي به تتعلق قدرة العبد وقيل الفعل المخلوق بقدرة الله من حيث خلق للعبد قدرة متعلقة به وقيل ما يقع به المقدور بلا صحة انفراد القادر. وما يقع في محل القدرة، والحق أنه ظاهر والخفاء في التعبير، والأوضح أنه أمر إضافي يجب من العبد، ولا يوجب وجود المقدور، بل اتصاف الفاعل بالمقدور كتعيين أحد الطرفين ،وترجيحه، وصرف القدرة .
(۱) راجع ما كتبه ابن حزم في هذا الموضوع فهو في غاية الوجاهة ورده على عباد بن سليمان تلميذ هشام بن عمر و الغوطي الذي قال: إن الله تعالى لم يخلق الكفار لأنهم ناس وكفر معاً لكن خلق
أجسامهم دون كفرهم جـ ٣ ص ٥٤ وما بعدها .
By meaning. The fifth chapter: On His actions. He said: The fifth chapter on His actions, and it contains investigations (1). The first investigation: The act of the slave occurs by the power of God most high, and the slave has only acquisition (kasb). And the Mu'tazila: by the power of the slave it is valid, and the philosophers: necessitation, and al-Ustadh: both of them together attached to it, and al-Qadi: that the power of God attaches to its origin and the power of the slave to its description like its being obedience or disobedience. As for jabr (compulsion), that there is no effect for the slave's power at all, neither creation nor acquisition, it is necessarily false. And al-kasb before that: the description by which the power of the slave attaches, and it is said: the act created by God's power insofar as He created for the slave a power related to it, and it is said: that by which the intended object occurs without the independent validity of the agent. And what occurs in the locus of power. And the truth is that it is apparent, and the obscurity is in the expression. And the clearest is that it is a relational matter necessitated from the slave, and does not cause the existence of the intended object, but rather the description of the agent by the intended object, like determining one of the two alternatives, and giving it preponderance, and directing the power. (1) Refer to what Ibn Hazm wrote on this subject, for it is extremely sound, and his refutation of 'Abbad ibn Sulayman, the student of Hisham ibn 'Amr al-Ghuti, who said that God most high did not create the disbelievers because they are people and disbelief together, but He created their bodies without their disbelief, vol. 3, p. 54 and after.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage opens the fifth chapter on God's actions and immediately addresses the first investigation (mabḥath): the human act. The author states the Ashʿarī position: the human act occurs by God's power, and the human has only acquisition (kasb). He then contrasts this with other views: the Muʿtazila affirm that the act is valid by the human's own power; the philosophers (ḥukamāʾ) hold that it is necessitated (ījāb); al-Ustādh (Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī) says both powers attach together; al-Qāḍī (al-Bāqillānī) distinguishes God's power attaching to the act's essence and human power attaching to its quality (e.g., obedience or disobedience). The author then dismisses pure determinism (jabr) as necessarily false—since it denies any effect of human power whatsoever, whether in creation or acquisition. Next, he defines kasb (acquisition) through three interpretations: (1) the description by which human power attaches to the act; (2) the act created by God's power insofar as He creates for the human a power related to it; (3) that by which the intended object (maqdūr) occurs without the independent validity of the agent—i.e., the human does not create independently. He notes that the concept is intuitively clear, but the difficulty lies in its verbal expression. The clearest explanation, he says, is that kasb is a relational matter (amr iḍāfī) that is necessitated by the human but does not cause the existence of the act; rather, it is the attribution of the act to the human agent—like specifying one of two alternatives, giving it preponderance, and directing the power. The footnote directs the reader to Ibn Ḥazm's refutation of ʿAbbād ibn Sulaymān, who denied that God creates the disbelief of disbelievers.

Key Terms
الكسب
وصف
الفعل المخلوق
المقدور
الإضافي
تعيين أحد الطرفين
ترجيح
صرف القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think kasb means the human co-creates the act with God.
Correction: The author explicitly says kasb does not cause existence; it is only the attribution of the act to the human.

Checkpoint
According to the author's preferred explanation, what is kasb?
A) The human's independent creation of the act.
B) A relational matter that attributes the act to the human without causing its existence.
C) The divine power that creates the act.
D) The negation of any human role in the act.

Key Takeaway
Kasb is a relational concept that safeguards divine creative power while allowing human moral responsibility through acquisition, not creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you perform—every word, every thought—is ultimately created by God, yet you are held accountable for it? How does that change the way you evaluate your successes and failures? Take a moment to recall a sin you committed. Did you attribute that act to your own 'power' or did you recognize that your ability to choose and act was given by God? How can you genuinely repent if you forget that kasb means you are described by the act but did not create it?
?
Your greatest act of worship—did you create it or acquire it? If you acquired it, why do you feel pride? If it was God's creation through you, why do you feel you deserve reward? Write down one act you are proud of and trace its existence back to God's creation and your acquisition. Let that reflection humble you and increase your gratitude.
?
Consider the phrase 'determining one of the two alternatives, giving it preponderance, and directing the power.' When you face a choice between obedience and disobedience, do you consciously direct your power toward what pleases God? Or do you let your desires determine the preponderance? What is one specific choice today where you can actively 'give preponderance' to obedience, acknowledging that God gives you the power to do so?

The Ash'ari Doctrine of Kasb: Human Acquisition, Divine Creation
The author states the Ash'ari doctrine: actions occur by God's power, humans only acquire them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari teaching that all human actions are created by God and that humans only 'acquire' them, clarifying the concept of kasb.

Core Idea
God creates every action; humans merely acquire responsibility.

This lesson presents the core Ash'ari position on human action in a single, precise statement. The author declares that the servant's action occurs solely through God's power, and the servant's role is limited to 'acquisition' (kasb). This distinction is vital for understanding how Ash'aris reconcile divine omnipotence with human moral accountability. Let's unpack this dense sentence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic phrase and its English translation. Notice the two parts: the first affirms God's power, the second limits the servant's role to kasb.
The term 'المبحث الأول' indicates this is the first section of a discussion.
2
Understand the theological stakes
Ask yourself: Why would it be problematic to say humans create their own actions? How does the Ash'ari position differ from the Mu'tazilite view that humans have independent power?
This lesson is about the doctrine, not the debate.
3
Define 'kasb' in Ash'ari terms
Consider what 'acquisition' means: the servant's voluntary choice is concurrent with God's creation of the action. The servant does not cause the action but acquires it by intending it.
The term 'kasb' is technical; later theologians elaborated on it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول
فعل العبد واقع بقدرة الله تعالى وإنما للعبد الكسب
The First Section: The action of the servant occurs by the power of Allah Almighty, and the servant only has acquisition.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a concise statement of the Ash'ari position on human actions. It first declares that the servant's action (فعل العبد) takes place by the power of God (بقدرة الله تعالى), affirming that divine omnipotence is the sole efficient cause of all events, including human acts. Then it adds that the servant has only 'al-kasb' (الكسب). The term 'kasb' is a technical term in Ash'ari theology, referring to the servant's acquisition of the action—a concurrent act of will and intention that is not creative but allows for moral responsibility. Thus, the passage succinctly presents the doctrine that God creates the action, and the human acquires it.

Correction: In Ash'ari theology, 'kasb' does not imply creation; it is a secondary acquisition concurrent with God's exclusive creation.

Checkpoint

C) The servant only has acquisition (kasb).
D) The servant's actions are independent of God's power.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari doctrine affirms that God creates every human action, and humans are responsible only through acquisition (kasb), not through independent power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Make a concrete plan to increase your sincere intention (niyyah) in one action today, recognizing that the outcome is from Allah.
?
The Ash'ari doctrine balances divine omnipotence and human responsibility. Reflect on your own life: Do you often credit yourself for successes or blame yourself too harshly for failures? How does this teaching reshape your view of your actions? Write down one area where you need to attribute your success more to Allah and take less pride.

The Servant's Action and Divine Power
Author introduces the topic of God's power and human acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opening thesis of the First Investigation: that human actions are created by God and humans only have acquisition (kasb).

Core Idea
God creates all actions; humans acquire them.

This lesson introduces a foundational thesis in Sunni theology on the relationship between divine power and human action. The author begins a new investigation by stating two key points: action belongs to God's power, and humans have only 'acquisition.' This sets the stage for the classical theory of kasb, which upholds both divine omnipotence and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice that it is a heading followed by a concise thesis statement.
The passage is the first sentence of a new investigation.
2
Identify the two claims
The passage makes two claims: (1) that the action of the servant is brought about by God's power, and (2) that the servant only has 'acquisition.' Understand them separately.
3
Explore the meaning of 'kasb'
The term 'kasb' (acquisition) is central. It refers to the human role of appropriating an action without creating it. Consider how this preserves both divine sovereignty and moral accountability.
This concept is key in Ash'ari theology.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
One might think this denies human responsibility. The passage guards against that by introducing 'kasb,' which allows for moral responsibility while affirming God's sole creative power.

The Principle: Acts Are Attributed to the Performer, Not the Creator
Defines the issue of creation of human acts with a clarifying analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why acts are attributed to the one who performs them, even though God creates them, and how this refutes the Qadariyya claim that humans create their own acts.

Core Idea
Acts belong to the doer, not the creator; whiteness inheres in the body, not in God.

This lesson tackles a central issue in the debate on human free will: if God creates all acts, why are acts attributed to humans? The author clarifies with a simple analogy: whiteness is created by God yet attributed to the body. Similarly, human acts are created by God but attributed to humans because they inhere in them. The Qadariyya's appeal to scriptural attributions misses this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Note the key terms and the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the analogy of whiteness – it is central.
2
Analyze the analogy
Identify how the analogy of whiteness and the body illustrates the principle: the attribute (whiteness) is ascribed to the subject (body) even though God creates it. Apply this to human acts.
Why does the author say there is no wonder in the common people's confusion, but wonder at the scholars'?
3
Examine the Qadariyya error
The passage states that the Qadariyya misuse scriptural verses that attribute acts to humans to argue that humans create their own acts. Understand why this is a mistake.
The scriptural attributions only show that the act inheres in the human, not that the human creates it.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider how this principle preserves divine omnipotence while maintaining human responsibility. The act is truly the human's act, but its existence is from God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولها في خلق أفعال العباد (۱) بمعنى أنه هل من جملة أفعال الله تعالى خلق الأفعال الاختيارية التي للعباد؟ بل لسائر الأحياء مع الاتفاق على أنها أفعالهم لا أفعاله، إذ القائم والقاعد والأكل والشارب وغير ذلك هو الإنسان مثلاً، وإن كان الفعل مخلوقاً لله تعالى، فإن الفعل إنما يستند حقيقة إلى من قام به لا إلى من أوجده، ألا ترى أن الأبيض مثلاً هو الجسم، وإن كان البياض بخلق الله وإيجاده، ولا عجب في خفاء هذا المعنى على عوام القدرية وجهالهم، حتى شنعوا به على أهل الحق في الأسواق، وإنما العجب خفاؤه على خواصهم وعلمائهم حتى سودوا به الصحائف والأوراق، وبهذا يظهر أن تمسكهم بما ورد في الكتاب والسنة من إسناد الأفعال إلى العباد لا يثبت المدعى وهو كون فعل العبد واقعاً بقدرته مخلوقاً له
Firstly, on the creation of the acts of the servants (1), meaning: is the creation of the voluntary acts of the servants among the acts of God Almighty? Rather, also for other living beings, with agreement that they are their acts, not His acts, since standing, sitting, eating, drinking, and other things are, for example, the human, although the act is created by God Almighty, for the act is truly attributed to the one in whom it inheres, not to the one who brings it into existence. Do you not see that, for example, the white thing is the body, although the whiteness is by God's creation and bringing into existence. And there is no wonder in the concealment of this meaning from the common people and the ignorant of the Qadariyya, such that they used it to slander the people of truth in the markets; rather, the wonder is its concealment from their elite and scholars, such that they have filled pages and papers with it. And by this it becomes clear that their adherence to what is reported in the Book and the Sunnah of attributing acts to the servants does not prove the claim, which is that the servant's act occurs by his own power and is created by him.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage defines the topic of the creation of human acts. The author clarifies that acts are attributed to the being that performs them, not to the one who brings them into existence, using the example of whiteness: the body is white, but the whiteness is created by God. Similarly, human actions are performed by humans, but the creation of the act is from God. He then criticizes the Qadariyya for misunderstanding this principle and for their public attacks, and points out that their appeal to scriptural attributions of acts to humans does not prove that humans create their own acts.

Correction: Scriptural attribution only indicates that the act inheres in the human, not that the human brings it into existence. Creation is solely from God.

Acts are attributed to the one who performs them, not to the one who creates them; even though all acts are created by God, they truly belong to the beings in which they inhere.

The Five Positions on Human Acts: Divine and Human Power
Enumerates the five views on divine and human power in bringing about human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five traditional positions on the roles of divine and human power in causing human actions.

Core Idea
Five schools divide the role of power in human acts between God and servant.

This passage from al-Mawaqif presents a concise survey of the major theological positions on how human acts come about. Each school—Ash'ari, Mu'tazila, al-Ustaz, al-Qadi, and the philosophers—assigns a different role to divine and human power. Reading this enumeration will help you see the key disagreements in qadar.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the five positions. Note the names: عندنا, المعتزلة, الأستاذ, القاضي, الحكماء.
2
Identify each view
For each school, identify what they say about the servant's act and which power(s) are effective.
Pay attention to the phrasing: بقدرة الله وحدها, بقدرة العبد وحدها, etc.
3
Note the critique
The author then argues that the Mu'tazila view is not distinct from the philosophers because both affirm that human power is created by God. Understand why this critique is made.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why these distinctions matter for broader theological debates about free will and divine omnipotence.
This connects to the core of qadar discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحرير المبحث على ما هو في المواقف .(٢) . إن فعل العبد واقع عندنا بقدرة الله وحدها. وعند المعتزلة بقدرة العبد وحدها، وعند الأستاذ بمجموع القدرتين على أن يتعلقا جميعاً بأصل الفعل، وعند القاضي على أن تتعلق قدرة الله تعالى بأصل الفعل، وقدرة العبد بكونه طاعة أو معصية. وعند الحكماء بقدرة يخلقها الله تعالى في العبد، ولا نزاع للمعتزلة في أن قدرة العبد مخلوقة الله تعالى وشاع في كلامهم أنه خالق القوى والقدر، فلا يمتاز مذهبهم عن مذهب الحكماء، ولا يفيد ما أشار إليه في المواقف من أن المؤثر عندهم قدرة العبد وعند الحكماء مجموع القدرتين (۳) على أن تتعلق قدرة الله بقدرة العبد وهي بالفعل.
And the refinement of the investigation according to what is in al-Mawaqif (2). The servant's act occurs in our view by God's power alone. According to the Mu'tazila, by the servant's power alone. According to al-Ustaz, by the combination of the two powers, both attaching to the act itself. According to al-Qadi, God's power attaches to the act itself, and the servant's power attaches to its being obedience or disobedience. According to the philosophers, by a power that God creates in the servant. And there is no dispute with the Mu'tazila that the servant's power is created by God Almighty, and it is widespread in their speech that He is the Creator of forces and power, so their school is not distinguished from the school of the philosophers, and what is indicated in al-Mawaqif is of no avail – that the effective cause according to them is the servant's power, while according to the philosophers it is the combination of the two powers (3) such that God's power attaches to the servant's power, and it is actual.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This paragraph enumerates the five positions on the relationship between divine and human power in bringing about human acts. The author presents the view of the Ash'aris (that only God's power is effective), the Mu'tazila (only human power), al-Ustaz (both powers together on the act itself), al-Qadi (God's power on the act itself, human power on its being obedience or disobedience), and the philosophers (God creates power in the human). Then he notes that the Mu'tazila claim that human power is created by God, so their view is not distinct from the philosophers, and the point in al-Mawaqif that the effective cause for them is human power while for the philosophers it is the combination is not helpful.

Misconception: The Mu'tazila claim that human acts are created by humans, but they also affirm that power is created by God, leading to an inconsistency that makes their school indistinguishable from the philosophers.
Correction: The author argues that since both Mu'tazila and philosophers hold that human power is created by God, any distinction based on which power is the effective cause is insufficient to separate them.

The Mu'tazila and Philosophers on Human Acts
Imam al-Razi's analysis of Mu'tazila and philosopher views on human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila and philosophers differ on human acts, specifically regarding the roles of soundness, choice, and necessitation.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila and philosophers agree on human causation but differ on how power is created.

This passage delves into a subtle but important distinction within Islamic theology: the relationship between human power and divine creation. Imam al-Razi and scholars after him clarify that while the Mu'tazila and philosophers share a common framework for human acts as necessitated by complete power and will, they diverge on the mode of God's creation of that power. This lesson unpacks that nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of technical terms like 'sihha' and 'ijab'.
2
Identify the two positions
Locate where the passage describes the Mu'tazila view (soundness and choice) and the philosophers' view (necessitation). Note the key phrases: 'على سبيل الصحة والاختيار' and 'على سبيل الإيجاب'.
3
Understand the distinction on soundness
The author says soundness is relative to power, but relative to complete power and will, it is only necessitation. Ask yourself: why does this matter?
This shows that the two views are not as different as they seem.
4
Note the muhaqqiq's conclusion
The passage states that the muhaqqiq declared the schools to be the same. What does this imply about the relationship between the Mu'tazila and philosophers?
They agree on the causal chain but differ on the divine origin of power.
5
Reflect on the remaining difference
The final sentence highlights the difference: Mu'tazila say God creates power by choice, philosophers say by necessitation due to complete preparedness. How does this affect human freedom?
The passage says necessitation does not negate choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر الإمام الرازي وتبعه بعض المعتزلة أن العبد عندهم موجد لأفعاله على سبيل الصحة والاختيار وعند الحكماء على سبيل الإيجاب بمعنى أن الله تعالى يوجب للعبد القدرة والإرادة ثم هما يوجبان وجود المقدور، وأنت خبير بأن الصحة إنما هي بالقياس إلى القدرة وأما بالقياس إلى تمام القدرة والإرادة فليس إلا الوجوب، وأنه لا ينافي الاختيار، ولهذا صرح المحقق في قواعد العقائيد أن هذا مذهب المعتزلة والحكماء جميعاً. نعم إن إيجاد (۱) القوى والقدر عند المعتزلة بطريق الاختيار، وعند الحكماء بطريق الإيجاب لتمام الاستعداد
And Imam al-Razi mentioned, and some Mu'tazila followed him, that the servant according to them is the bringer-into-existence of his acts in the manner of soundness and choice, while according to the philosophers it is in the manner of necessitation, meaning that God Almighty necessitates for the servant power and will, then they both necessitate the existence of the object of power. And you are aware that soundness relates relative to power, but relative to complete power and will, there is only necessitation, and it does not negate choice. For this reason, the muhaqqiq declared in Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id that this is the school of the Mu'tazila and the philosophers all together. Yes, the bringing-into-existence of forces and power according to the Mu'tazila is by way of choice, and according to the philosophers by way of necessitation due to complete preparedness.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage discusses the nuance between the Mu'tazila and philosophers. Imam al-Razi and some Mu'tazila say that the servant brings his acts into existence in a mode of soundness and choice, while the philosophers say it is by necessitation (ijab): God necessitates power and will, and they necessitate the act. The author points out that 'soundness' (sihha) is relative to power, but relative to complete power and will, it is only necessitation, and this does not contradict choice. Therefore, the muhaqqiq states that the two schools are essentially the same, differing only in that the Mu'tazila view the creation of power as by choice, while the philosophers see it as by necessitation due to complete preparedness.

المحقق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the philosophers' view of necessitation (ijab) denies human free will entirely.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that necessitation does not negate choice, because the act is still performed voluntarily by the servant.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'الصحة' (soundness) refer to in the context of human power?
A) The act is possible
B) The act is sound
C) Relative to power it means possibility, but relative to complete power and will it means necessity
D) The act is optional

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila and philosophers agree that human acts are necessitated by complete power and will, but differ on whether God creates that power by choice or by necessitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you truly believe that your complete power and will necessitate the act, or do you sometimes feel as though your actions are independent of God's creation? How does this lesson challenge that feeling? Write down one action today where you will consciously remind yourself that your ability to act is created by God.
?
The author says necessitation does not negate choice. But do you live as though your choices are truly yours, or do you sometimes attribute your sins to God's decree? Take a moment to repent for any such thought and affirm that you are responsible for your actions.
?
Consider a time when you had complete power and will to do something good, yet you hesitated. What was missing? Was it truly complete will? Reflect on the fact that even your will is created by God. Will you thank Him for granting you the will to obey, and ask Him for steadfastness?

Imam al-Haramayn's True Stance on Human Acts
Presents Imam al-Haramayn's quoted position from al-Irshad and the author's summary of his refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Haramayn's actual position on human acts as stated in his book al-Irshad, correcting the common misconception that he followed the philosophers, and see his refutation of Mu'tazila and Jabriyya along with his affirmation of kasb.

Core Idea
Imam al-Haramayn rejects both human autonomy and coercion, affirming God's exclusive creativity and a non-effective human power.

Many later theologians attribute to Imam al-Haramayn the view that human acts are necessitated by human power (like the philosophers). But his own work al-Irshad reveals a different doctrine. In this lesson, we examine the passage where he clarifies his agreement with the Salaf that God alone is the Creator and human power has no effect, and then his refutation of opposing sects and affirmation of kasb.

Guided Path
1
Identify the misconception
Read the beginning of the passage where the author states the well-known but false attribution of Imam al-Haramayn's view to the philosophers.
Note that this is what 'the people' say, not what the Imam himself said.
2
Read the direct quote from al-Irshad
Carefully read the quote from Imam al-Haramayn's al-Irshad, where he affirms the Salaf's consensus that God alone is the Creator and all events occur by His power.
Notice the key point: attachment of an attribute does not imply causal effect.
3
Understand the critique of Mu'tazila
See how the Imam distinguishes the earlier Mu'tazila (who refrained from calling the slave a creator) from the later ones (who did), all while rejecting their idea that humans bring actions into existence.
4
Observe the refutation of both sects
After the quote, the author summarizes that Imam al-Haramayn presented proofs, answered Mu'tazila doubts, and refuted both Mu'tazila and Jabriyya.
The Imam's position avoids both extremes.
5
Grasp the affirmation of kasb
Finally, note that the Imam affirmed for the servant a kasb (acquisition) and a power that accompanies the act but has no effect on it.
This power is non-effective; only God's power creates.

ثم المشهور فيما بين القوم والمذكور في كتبهم أن مذهب إمام الحرمين، أن فعل العبد واقع بقدرته وإرادته إيجاباً (٢) كما هو رأي الحكماء، وهذا خلاف ما صرح به الإمام فيما وقع إلينا من كتبه، قال في الإرشاد: اتفق أئمة السلف قبل ظهور البدع والأهواء على أن الخالق هو الله. ولا خالق سواه، وأن الحوادث كلها حدثت بقدرة الله تعالى من غير فرق بين ما يتعلق قدرة العباد به، وبين ما لا يتعلق، فإن تعلق الصفة بشيء لا يستلزم تأثيرها فيه كالعلم بالمعلوم والإرادة بفعل الغير فالقدرة الحادثة لا توثر في مقدورها أضلاً، واتفقت المعتزلة ومن تابعهم من أهل الزيغ على أن العباد موجدون لأفعالهم، مخترعون لها بقدرهم ثم المتقدمون منهم كانوا يمنعون من تسمية العبد خالقاً لقرب عهدهم بإجماع السلف على أنه لا خالق إلا الله. واجترأ المتأخرون فسموا العبد خالقاً على الحقيقة هذا كلامه، ثم أورد أدلة الأصحاب، وأجاب عن شبه المعتزلة، وبالغ في الرد عليهم وعلى الجبرية (۳)، وأثبت للعبد كسباً وقدرة مقارنة للفعل غير مؤثرة فيه، وأما الأستاذ فإن أراد أن قدرة العبد غير مستقلة بالتأثير، وإذا انضمت إليها قدرة الله تعالى صارت
Then what is well-known among the people and mentioned in their books is that the school of Imam al-Haramayn is that the servant's act occurs by his power and will by way of necessitation (2), as is the opinion of the philosophers. And this is contrary to what the Imam explicitly stated in the books of his that have reached us. He said in al-Irshad: The imams of the Salaf before the emergence of innovations and desires agreed that the Creator is God, and no creator other than Him, and that all events occurred by the power of God Almighty, with no difference between what the servants' power pertains to and what it does not pertain to. For the attachment of an attribute to something does not imply its effect in it, like knowledge of the known and will regarding the act of another. So the created power has no effect at all in its object. And the Mu'tazila and those who followed them from the people of deviation agreed that the servants are bringers-into-existence of their acts, inventors of them by their power. Then the earlier ones among them refrained from calling the servant a creator due to the proximity of their era to the consensus of the Salaf that there is no creator but God. The later ones became bold and called the servant a creator in reality. This is his statement. Then he presented the proofs of the followers, answered the doubts of the Mu'tazila, and went to great length in refuting them and the Jabriyya (3), and affirmed for the servant a kasb (acquisition) and a power accompanying the act without being effective in it. And as for al-Ustaz, if he means that the servant's power is not independent in effect, and when God's power joins it, it becomes...
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents the famous view that Imam al-Haramayn's position is like the philosophers (acts occur by the slave's power and will necessarily), but then quotes from his al-Irshad to show he actually agrees with the Salaf: God alone is the Creator, all events happen by His power, and the created power has no effect whatsoever. The Mu'tazila claimed that humans create their own acts; early ones refrained from calling the slave a creator, later ones boldly did so. Imam al-Haramayn then provided proofs and refuted the Mu'tazila and the Jabriyya, affirming a kasb (acquisition) and a power that accompanies the act without causing it. Finally, the author discusses al-Ustaz's view that human power is not independent, and when joined with God's power it becomes effective (the sentence is cut off at the end of the page).

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Haramayn affirms that God alone is the Creator, human power has no causal effect, and the servant's act is an acquisition (kasb) accompanied by a non-effective power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you truly believe that God created that action? Or do you feel pride as if you are the one who produced it? Reflect on a recent act of worship—where did your attribution go? Commit to reminding yourself that your power is only a means, not a cause.
?
The Mu'tazila called the servant a creator. In your daily life, do you ever subconsciously think of yourself as the 'creator' of your achievements? How would acknowledging God as the sole Creator change your humility and gratitude? Write a specific prayer you can say before any action.
?
Imam al-Haramayn's stance is a middle path between autonomy and coercion. In your own struggles with sin, do you swing between blaming yourself (like Mu'tazila) or blaming God (like Jabriyya)? How can the concept of kasb help you take responsibility without despair? Make a concrete plan to apply this to one recurring sin.

Mu'tazila and Philosophers on Human Action
Comparison of Mu'tazila and philosopher views on human action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila and philosopher views on human action, and why necessitation (ījāb) does not contradict free choice.

Core Idea
Necessitation and choice are compatible in kalam discussions of human action.

The passage opens by contrasting two positions: the Mu'tazila hold that servants originate their actions with soundness and choice, while the philosophers hold that God necessitates power and will in the servant, which then necessitate the action. The author clarifies that soundness is only relative to power, but with complete power and will, necessitation occurs—yet this does not negate choice. This nuanced claim is supported by a citation from al-Muḥaqqiq.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the full Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the author's wording. Notice the key terms: ikhtiyār, ījāb, qudra.
The passage uses technical terms that are defined in context.
2
Identify the two positions
The author attributes one view to the Mu'tazila (and Imam al-Razi) and another to the philosophers (ḥukamā'). Compare their claims about origination and necessitation.
3
Understand the compatibility claim
The author argues that necessitation (ījāb) does not contradict choice (ikhtiyār). Reflect on why this might be a subtle point about causality and free will.
This is a key insight that reconciles divine predestination with human responsibility.
4
Note the supplementary clarification
The last sentence of the passage distinguishes between the Mu'tazila and philosophers regarding the origination of powers (choice vs. necessitation due to preparedness). This adds depth to the comparison.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي بالفعل. وذكر الإمام الرازي وتبعه بعض المعتزلة أن العبد عندهم موجد لأفعاله على سبيل الصحة والاختيار وعند الحكماء على سبيل الإيجاب بمعنى أن الله تعالى يوجب للعبد القدرة والإرادة ثم هما يوجبان وجود المقدور، وأنت خبير بأن الصحة إنما هي بالقياس إلى القدرة وأما بالقياس إلى تمام القدرة والإرادة فليس إلا الوجوب، وأنه لا ينافي الاختيار، ولهذا صرح المحقق في قواعد العقائيد أن هذا مذهب المعتزلة والحكماء جميعاً. نعم إن إيجاد (۱) القوى والقدر عند المعتزلة بطريق الاختيار، وعند الحكماء بطريق الإيجاب لتمام الاستعداد ثم المشهور فيما بين القوم والمذكور في كتبهم أن مذهب إمام الحرمين، أن فعل العبد واقع بقدرته وإرادته إيجاباً (۲) كما هو رأي الحكماء، وهذا خلاف ما صرح به الإمام فيما وقع إلينا من كتبه، قال في الإرشاد : اتفق أئمة السلف قبل ظهور البدع والأهواء على أن الخالق هو الله. ولا خالق سواه، وأن الحوادث كلها حدثت بقدرة الله تعالى من غير فرق بين ما يتعلق قدرة العباد به، وبين ما لا يتعلق، فإن تعلق الصفة بشيء لا يستلزم تأثيرها فيه كالعلم بالمعلوم والإرادة بفعل الغير فالقدرة الحادثة لا توثر في مقدورها أضلاً، واتفقت المعتزلة ومن تابعهم من أهل الزيغ على أن العباد موجدون لأفعالهم، مخترعون لها بقدرهم ثم المتقدمون منهم كانوا يمنعون من تسمية العبد خالقاً لقرب عهدهم بإجماع السلف على أنه لا خالق إلا الله . واجترأ المتأخرون فسموا العبد خالقاً على الحقيقة هذا كلامه، ثم أورد أدلة الأصحاب، وأجاب عن شبه المعتزلة، وبالغ في الرد عليهم وعلى الجبرية (۳) ، وأثبت للعبد كسباً وقدرة مقارنة للفعل غير مؤثرة فيه، وأما الأستاذ فإن أراد أن قدرة العبد غير مستقلة بالتأثير، وإذا انضمت إليها قدرة الله تعالى صارت مستقلة بالتأثير بتوسط هذه الإعانة على ما قدره البعض، فقريب من الحق، وإن أراد أن كلاً من القدرتين مستقلة بالتأثير فباطل لما سبق. وكذا الجبر المطلق، وهو أن أفعال الحيوانات بمنزلة حركات الجمادات لا تتعلق بها قدرتها لا إيجاداً ولا كسباً، وذلك لما نجد من الفرق الضروري بين حركة المرتعش وحركة الماشي، فبقي الكلام بين الكسبية والقدرية.
And it is actual. And Imam al-Razi mentioned, and some Mu'tazila followed him, that the servant according to them is the one who originates his actions in the way of soundness and choice, and according to the philosophers in the way of necessitation, meaning that God Most High necessitates for the servant power and will, then they both necessitate the existence of the intended thing. And you are well aware that soundness is only in relation to the power, but in relation to the complete power and will, there is only necessitation, and that it does not contradict choice. For this reason, the verifier stated in Qawā‘id al-‘Aqā’id that this is the doctrine of the Mu‘tazila and the philosophers all together. Yes, the bringing into existence of the powers and capability for the Mu‘tazila is by way of choice, and for the philosophers by way of necessitation due to complete preparedness. Then the well-known among the people and mentioned in their books is that the doctrine of Imam al-Ḥaramayn is that the action of the servant occurs by his power and will necessarily as is the opinion of the philosophers, and this is contrary to what the Imam explicitly stated in what has reached us of his books. He said in al-Irshād: 'The early imams before the appearance of innovations and desires agreed that the Creator is God, and there is no creator other than Him, and that all events occur by the power of God Most High without distinction between what pertains to the power of servants and what does not. For the attachment of an attribute to something does not entail its effect in it, like knowledge of the known and will concerning the action of another. So the originated power does not affect its intended object at all. And the Mu‘tazila and those who followed them among the people of deviation agreed that servants are the originators of their actions, inventors of them by their power. Then the earlier ones among them used to forbid calling the servant 'creator' due to the proximity of their time to the consensus of the Salaf that there is no creator but God. And the later ones became audacious and called the servant 'creator' in reality.' This is his statement. Then he adduced the proofs of the companions and answered the specious arguments of the Mu‘tazila, and went to extremes in refuting them and the Jabriyya, and he affirmed for the servant acquisition and a power concomitant with the action, not effective in it. As for the master, if he means that the power of the servant is not independent in effect, and when the power of God Most High is joined to it, it becomes independent in effect through this assistance as some have determined, then it is close to the truth. And if he means that each of the two powers is independent in effect, then it is false due to what preceded. And likewise absolute jabr, which is that the actions of animals are like the movements of inanimate objects, not related to their power either in origination or acquisition, due to what we find of the necessary distinction between the movement of the tremulous person and the movement of the walker. So the remaining discussion is between the Kasbiyya and the Qadariyya.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage surveys different views on human action and power. It begins by stating that Imam al-Razi and some Mu'tazila hold that the servant originates his actions in a sound manner and with choice, while the philosophers hold that God necessitates power and will for the servant, which then necessitate the action, and this is compatible with choice. The author notes that soundness is relative to power alone, but with complete power and will, there is necessitation, yet it does not negate choice. He then cites al-Muḥaqqiq who says this is the doctrine of both Mu'tazila and philosophers. He clarifies that for Mu'tazila the origination of powers is by choice, for philosophers by necessitation due to complete preparedness. He discusses a common attribution to Imam al-Ḥaramayn that he agrees with the philosophers, but shows this contradicts what he wrote in al-Irshād. He quotes al-Irshād affirming that God is the sole creator and all events occur by His power, and that the originated power has no effect on its intended object. He mentions that the Mu'tazila later became audacious and called the servant a creator. Then he says that Imam al-Ḥaramayn affirmed kasb and a power that accompanies the action without being effective. He then discusses the opinion of 'al-Ustādh' regarding the independence of the servant's power when joined with God's power, rejecting the idea that both powers are independent. He also rejects absolute jabr which denies any relation of the servant's power to actions, citing the necessary distinction between involuntary and voluntary movements. He concludes that the remaining debate is between the Kasbiyya (Ash'aris who hold kasb) and the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila who hold that servants create their actions).

qudra

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessitation (ījāb) necessarily implies compulsion (jabr) and negates free will.
Correction: The author argues that necessitation does not contradict choice (ikhtiyār); the act can be both necessitated by causes and freely chosen by the agent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does al-Muḥaqqiq state in Qawā‘id al-‘Aqā’id?
A) The Mu'tazila and philosophers agree that the servant is the creator of his actions.
B) The doctrine of necessitation with choice is the view of both Mu'tazila and philosophers.
C) The philosophers deny that humans have any power over their actions.
D) The Mu'tazila hold that God directly creates all actions.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila and philosophers both allow that human actions are necessitated by divine power and will, yet this does not eliminate human choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you tend to blame your own weakness or feel that it was 'written'? How does the lesson's claim that necessitation does not contradict choice challenge your tendency, and what does that demand from you right now?
?
The passage says that when power and will are complete, the act is necessitated—yet you still call it 'choice'. Can you identify one recent decision where you felt both compelled by circumstances and freely responsible? How does this lesson's framework help you take full accountability without resentment?
?
If God's creativity of your power and will truly necessitates your actions, then in what sense can you claim to be the 'doer'? The author insists the servant originates acts 'on the basis of soundness and choice'—but if you're honest, do you live as if ultimate responsibility rests on you or on God? What one change would living by this lesson's conclusion require tomorrow morning?

Imam al-Ḥaramayn on Kasb and the Final Debate
Quotes Imam al-Ḥaramayn, discusses al-Ustādh, rejects absolute jabr, concludes with Kasbiyya vs. Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Ḥaramayn's position on human action as stated in al-Irshād, the rejection of absolute jabr, and the final dichotomy between Kasbiyya and Qadariyya.

Core Idea
The true debate on human action is between the Kasbiyya (Ash'aris) and the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila).

After surveying earlier views, the passage turns to Imam al-Ḥaramayn al-Juwaynī, a key Ashʿarī authority. It corrects a common misattribution by quoting his book al-Irshād, where he affirms that God alone creates all events, and that originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) has no effect. He then discusses the views of 'al-Ustādh' regarding whether the servant's power becomes independent when joined with God's power, and rejects absolute jabr. The passage concludes by narrowing the discussion to two rival schools: the Kasbiyya (those who affirm acquisition) and the Qadariyya (those who affirm that servants create their actions).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from al-Irshād
Focus on the quoted segment from al-Irshād. Notice how it emphatically asserts that God is the sole creator and that all events occur by His power, with no distinction.
This quote is a primary source for the Ash'ari stance.
2
Understand the denial of effect in originated power
The passage says that the attachment of an attribute to something does not entail its effect. For example, God's knowledge of an object does not affect it. Similarly, originated power (human power) does not affect its supposed object.
This is the core of the kasb doctrine: human power is concurrent with the act but not effective.
3
Examine al-Ustādh's two possibilities
al-Ustādh (likely a reference to Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyinī or similar) proposes that either the servant's power is not independent and becomes so only with God's assistance, or both powers are independently effective. The author rejects the latter.
This clarifies the range of Ash'ari thought on the matter.
4
Reflect on the rejection of absolute jabr
The author argues that absolute jabr (denying any relation of the servant's power to actions) is false because there is a necessary distinction between involuntary and voluntary movement.
This preserves human moral responsibility while maintaining divine omnipotence.
5
Find the concluding dichotomy
The last sentence says the remaining discussion is between the Kasbiyya and the Qadariyya. This is the key takeaway: the real debate is not with philosophers or jabrites, but between these two positions.

وهي بالفعل. وذكر الإمام الرازي وتبعه بعض المعتزلة أن العبد عندهم موجد لأفعاله على سبيل الصحة والاختيار وعند الحكماء على سبيل الإيجاب بمعنى أن الله تعالى يوجب للعبد القدرة والإرادة ثم هما يوجبان وجود المقدور، وأنت خبير بأن الصحة إنما هي بالقياس إلى القدرة وأما بالقياس إلى تمام القدرة والإرادة فليس إلا الوجوب، وأنه لا ينافي الاختيار، ولهذا صرح المحقق في قواعد العقائيد أن هذا مذهب المعتزلة والحكماء جميعاً. نعم إن إيجاد (۱) القوى والقدر عند المعتزلة بطريق الاختيار، وعند الحكماء بطريق الإيجاب لتمام الاستعداد ثم المشهور فيما بين القوم والمذكور في كتبهم أن مذهب إمام الحرمين، أن فعل العبد واقع بقدرته وإرادته إيجاباً (۲) كما هو رأي الحكماء، وهذا خلاف ما صرح به الإمام فيما وقع إلينا من كتبه، قال في الإرشاد : اتفق أئمة السلف قبل ظهور البدع والأهواء على أن الخالق هو الله. ولا خالق سواه، وأن الحوادث كلها حدثت بقدرة الله تعالى من غير فرق بين ما يتعلق قدرة العباد به، وبين ما لا يتعلق، فإن تعلق الصفة بشيء لا يستلزم تأثيرها فيه كالعلم بالمعلوم والإرادة بفعل الغير فالقدرة الحادثة لا توثر في مقدورها أضلاً، واتفقت المعتزلة ومن تابعهم من أهل الزيغ على أن العباد موجدون لأفعالهم، مخترعون لها بقدرهم ثم المتقدمون منهم كانوا يمنعون من تسمية العبد خالقاً لقرب عهدهم بإجماع السلف على أنه لا خالق إلا الله . واجترأ المتأخرون فسموا العبد خالقاً على الحقيقة هذا كلامه، ثم أورد أدلة الأصحاب، وأجاب عن شبه المعتزلة، وبالغ في الرد عليهم وعلى الجبرية (۳) ، وأثبت للعبد كسباً وقدرة مقارنة للفعل غير مؤثرة فيه، وأما الأستاذ فإن أراد أن قدرة العبد غير مستقلة بالتأثير، وإذا انضمت إليها قدرة الله تعالى صارت مستقلة بالتأثير بتوسط هذه الإعانة على ما قدره البعض، فقريب من الحق، وإن أراد أن كلاً من القدرتين مستقلة بالتأثير فباطل لما سبق. وكذا الجبر المطلق، وهو أن أفعال الحيوانات بمنزلة حركات الجمادات لا تتعلق بها قدرتها لا إيجاداً ولا كسباً، وذلك لما نجد من الفرق الضروري بين حركة المرتعش وحركة الماشي، فبقي الكلام بين الكسبية والقدرية.
And it is actual. And Imam al-Razi mentioned, and some Mu'tazila followed him, that the servant according to them is the one who originates his actions in the way of soundness and choice, and according to the philosophers in the way of necessitation, meaning that God Most High necessitates for the servant power and will, then they both necessitate the existence of the intended thing. And you are well aware that soundness is only in relation to the power, but in relation to the complete power and will, there is only necessitation, and that it does not contradict choice. For this reason, the verifier stated in Qawā‘id al-‘Aqā’id that this is the doctrine of the Mu‘tazila and the philosophers all together. Yes, the bringing into existence of the powers and capability for the Mu‘tazila is by way of choice, and for the philosophers by way of necessitation due to complete preparedness. Then the well-known among the people and mentioned in their books is that the doctrine of Imam al-Ḥaramayn is that the action of the servant occurs by his power and will necessarily as is the opinion of the philosophers, and this is contrary to what the Imam explicitly stated in what has reached us of his books. He said in al-Irshād: 'The early imams before the appearance of innovations and desires agreed that the Creator is God, and there is no creator other than Him, and that all events occur by the power of God Most High without distinction between what pertains to the power of servants and what does not. For the attachment of an attribute to something does not entail its effect in it, like knowledge of the known and will concerning the action of another. So the originated power does not affect its intended object at all. And the Mu‘tazila and those who followed them among the people of deviation agreed that servants are the originators of their actions, inventors of them by their power. Then the earlier ones among them used to forbid calling the servant 'creator' due to the proximity of their time to the consensus of the Salaf that there is no creator but God. And the later ones became audacious and called the servant 'creator' in reality.' This is his statement. Then he adduced the proofs of the companions and answered the specious arguments of the Mu‘tazila, and went to extremes in refuting them and the Jabriyya, and he affirmed for the servant acquisition and a power concomitant with the action, not effective in it. As for the master, if he means that the power of the servant is not independent in effect, and when the power of God Most High is joined to it, it becomes independent in effect through this assistance as some have determined, then it is close to the truth. And if he means that each of the two powers is independent in effect, then it is false due to what preceded. And likewise absolute jabr, which is that the actions of animals are like the movements of inanimate objects, not related to their power either in origination or acquisition, due to what we find of the necessary distinction between the movement of the tremulous person and the movement of the walker. So the remaining discussion is between the Kasbiyya and the Qadariyya.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage surveys different views on human action and power. It begins by stating that Imam al-Razi and some Mu'tazila hold that the servant originates his actions in a sound manner and with choice, while the philosophers hold that God necessitates power and will for the servant, which then necessitate the action, and this is compatible with choice. The author notes that soundness is relative to power alone, but with complete power and will, there is necessitation, yet it does not negate choice. He then cites al-Muḥaqqiq who says this is the doctrine of both Mu'tazila and philosophers. He clarifies that for Mu'tazila the origination of powers is by choice, for philosophers by necessitation due to complete preparedness. He discusses a common attribution to Imam al-Ḥaramayn that he agrees with the philosophers, but shows this contradicts what he wrote in al-Irshād. He quotes al-Irshād affirming that God is the sole creator and all events occur by His power, and that the originated power has no effect on its intended object. He mentions that the Mu'tazila later became audacious and called the servant a creator. Then he says that Imam al-Ḥaramayn affirmed kasb and a power that accompanies the action without being effective. He then discusses the opinion of 'al-Ustādh' regarding the independence of the servant's power when joined with God's power, rejecting the idea that both powers are independent. He also rejects absolute jabr which denies any relation of the servant's power to actions, citing the necessary distinction between involuntary and voluntary movements. He concludes that the remaining debate is between the Kasbiyya (Ash'aris who hold kasb) and the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila who hold that servants create their actions).

qudra ḥāditha

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari doctrine of kasb means humans have no real role in their actions; it is a form of jabr.
Correction: The passage shows that kasb affirms a power that accompanies the action (though ineffective), and distinguishes between involuntary and voluntary actions, thus preserving moral responsibility.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the remaining discussion after examining various views?
A) Between the Mu'tazila and the Jabriyya
B) Between the philosophers and the Ash'aris
C) Between the Kasbiyya and the Qadariyya
D) Between the Mu'tazila and the philosophers

Key Takeaway
The essential debate on human action is between the Ash'ari Kasbiyya, who affirm a non-effective power, and the Mu'tazila Qadariyya, who affirm that servants create their actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever blamed God for a sin you committed, falling into a form of jabr? How does this passage challenge you to take responsibility for your actions while affirming God's sole creative power? In your next moment of failure, will you own your choice or shift the blame?
?
The passage distinguishes between involuntary tremors and voluntary walking. In your life, where do you need to honestly acknowledge your own voluntary choices rather than attributing them to divine compulsion? What specific action will you take today to exercise your God-given responsibility?
?
The early imams agreed that God is the sole creator, and the power of servants does not affect its object. How does this truth affect your sense of humility and reliance on God in your daily efforts? Write one concrete way you will consciously depend on God in your next task.
