Imam al-Razi's Definition of Kasb
Imam al-Razi defines kasb with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's definition of kasb and how it distinguishes the servant's contribution from Allah's creation in human actions.

Core Idea
Kasb is the servant's specific quality in an action while Allah creates the action itself.

In the debate over human responsibility, the term 'kasb' is often criticized as meaningless. This passage responds by first acknowledging the dispute between the Kasbiyya (Ash'aris) and Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) and then providing Imam al-Razi's precise definition. Al-Razi explains kasb using the example of prayer and killing: both are movements created by Allah, but their moral quality (obedience/disobedience) comes from the servant, and that quality is what is called kasb.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from context to definition.
The passage has two parts: setting up the problem and then al-Razi's answer.
2
Identify the Problem
Why does the author say it is necessary to explain the meaning of kasb? Look for the phrase 'to counter what is said that it is a name without a referent'.
The opponents claimed kasb is an empty term.
3
Examine the Two Perspectives
First, some Ahl al-Sunnah accept kasb based on proof and necessity without fully knowing its reality. Then Imam al-Razi gives a more specific definition. Compare the two.
Both emphasize that only Allah creates, but the servant's power has an effect.
4
Understand al-Razi's Definition and Example
Al-Razi defines kasb as a quality that occurs through the servant's power in an action that occurs by Allah's power. He illustrates with prayer and killing: both are movements, but one is obedience, the other disobedience. The shared element (movement) is from Allah; the distinguishing element (moral quality) is from the servant, and that is kasb.
This preserves divine omnipotence while accounting for human responsibility.
5
Reflect on the Significance
Ask yourself: How does this definition resolve the dispute? Does it satisfy both divine power and human accountability?
This definition is central to Ash'ari theology of action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فبقي الكلام بين الكسبية والقدرية. ولكن لا بد أولاً من بيان معنى الكسب دفعاً لما يقال إنه اسم بلا مسمى فاكتفى بعض أهل السنة، بأنا نعلم بالبرهان أن لا خالق سوى ولا تأثير إلا للقدرة القديمة، ونعلم بالضرورة أن القدرة الحادثة للعبد تتعلق ببعض أفعاله كالصعود دون البعض كالسقوط فيسمى أثر تعلق القدرة الحادثة كسباً وإن لم يعرف حقيقته. قال الإمام الرازي: هي صفة تحصل بقدرة العبد بفعله الحاصل بقدرة الله تعالى فإن الصلاة والقتل مثلاً كلاهما حركة، ويتمايزان بكون إحداهما طاعة، والأخرى معصية، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به التمايز فأصل الحركة بقدرة الله تعالى وخصوصية الوصف بقدرة العبد، وهي المسماة بالكسب.
The dispute remained between the Kasbiyya and the Qadariyya. But first it is necessary to explain the meaning of acquisition (al-kasb) to counter what is said that it is a name without a referent. Some of the Ahl al-Sunnah were content with [saying] that we know by proof that there is no creator other than [Allah] and no effect except through the eternal power, and we know necessarily that the originated power of the servant relates to some of his actions like ascending, not to others like falling, so the effect of the relation of the originated power is called acquisition even if its reality is not known. Imam al-Razi said: It is a quality that occurs through the power of the servant in his action that occurs by the power of Allah the Exalted. For example, prayer and killing are both movements, and they are distinguished by one being obedience and the other disobedience. That which is shared is other than that by which they differ. So the origin of the movement is by the power of Allah the Exalted, and the particularity of the description is by the power of the servant, and it is what is called acquisition.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage defines the term kasb (acquisition) and provides Imam al-Razi's definition. It begins by setting the context: the dispute between the Kasbiyya (Ash'aris) and Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) requires a clear meaning of kasb, because opponents say it is a name without a referent. The author notes that some Ahl al-Sunnah are satisfied with the proof that only Allah creates and that the human power relates to some actions, and the effect of that relation is called kasb even if its essence is unknown. Then Imam al-Razi gives a more precise definition: kasb is a quality that arises through the servant's power in an action that is created by Allah's power. He illustrates with prayer and killing: both are movements (shared element from Allah), but one is obedience and the other disobedience (distinguishing element from the servant). So the origin of movement is from Allah, and the specific description from the servant, and this combination is called kasb. This definition preserves divine omnipotence while accounting for human responsibility.

القدرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think kasb is just a meaningless term invented by Ash'aris with no real content.
Correction: The passage shows that kasb has a precise definition in Ash'ari theology: it is the specific quality (e.g., obedience or disobedience) that arises from the servant's power in an action whose substance is created by Allah.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi's definition, what is the distinguishing element between prayer and killing?
A) The origin of the movement
B) The power of Allah
C) The specific description (obedience or disobedience) from the servant's power
D) The fact that both are movements

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi defines kasb as the quality arising from the servant's power in an action created by Allah, thus reconciling divine omnipotence with human accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a specific sin you committed today. The movement itself was created by Allah, but the disobedience—the wrongness—came from your own power. Will you now repent with the full acknowledgment that the evil description was entirely yours, not Allah's?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you silently credit yourself for the obedience? Al-Razi teaches that the very quality of obedience is a result of your power—but why do you feel pride? How can you remain humble before the One who created the act itself?
?
If the substance of every action is from Allah, then you cannot blame circumstances or other people for your sins. The 'particularity of description' is from you. Will you today take full ownership of one moral failure and refuse to make excuses?

Three Definitions of Kasb and al-Ghazali's Synthesis
Presents three alternative definitions of Kasb and al-Ghazali's synthesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three alternative definitions of kasb and how al-Ghazali synthesized them into a coherent doctrine reconciling divine creation and human acquisition.

Core Idea
Kasb is a middle path between determinism and human self-creation.

This passage presents three earlier attempts to define kasb, followed by al-Ghazali's masterful synthesis. Each definition tries to articulate how human power relates to divine creation without falling into extremes. Understanding these nuances is key to grasping the Ash'ari position on free will and predestination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the distinct phrases 'وقريب من ذلك', 'وقيل', 'وقيل' marking three definitions.
2
Identify each definition
Locate the three definitions and note the key distinctions: origin vs. specification, creation of power alongside the act, and relational power only affecting attributions.
The third definition is the most precise Ash'ari view distinguishing kasb from khalq.
3
Analyze al-Ghazali's synthesis
Read the final paragraph carefully. Notice how al-Ghazali combines the valid elements: actions are created by Allah by invention (ikhtira') and acquired by the servant by a different mode of relation (iktisab).
He uses the example of Allah's eternal relation to the world to illustrate two modes of relation.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this view preserves both divine omnipotence and human moral responsibility. The act is created by Allah, yet the servant is truly the agent of acquisition, which grounds moral attributions.
This answers the problem of evil: evil is in the acquisition, not in the creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقريب من ذلك ما يقال أن أصل الحركة بقدرة الله وتعينها بقدرة العبد وهو الكسب وفيه نظر. وقيل الفعل الذي يخلقه الله تعالى في العبد، ويخلق معه قدرة للعبد متعلقة به يسمى كسباً للعبد بخلاف ما إذا لم يخلق معه تلك القدرة. وقيل إن للعبد قدرة تختلف بها النسب والإضافات فقط كتعيين أحد طرفي الفعل والترك وترجيحه، ولا يلزم منها وجود أمر فالأمر الإضافي الذي يجب من العبد، ولا يجب عند وجود الأثر هو الكسب، وهذا ما قالوا هو ما يقع به المقدور بلا صحة انفراد القادرية وما يقع في محل قدرته بخلاف الخلق فإنه ما يقع به المقدور مع صحة انفراد القادرية وما يقع لا في محل قدرته، فالكسب لا يوجب وجود المقدور بل يوجب من حيث هو كسب اتصاف الفاعل بذلك المقدور، ولهذا يكون مرجعاً لاختلاف الإضافات ككون الفعل طاعة أو معصية حسناً أو قبيحاً، فالاتصاف بالقبيح بقصده وإرادته قبيح بخلاف خلق القبيح فإنه لا ينافي المصلحة والعاقبة الحميدة بل ربما يشتمل عليها. وملخص الكلام ما أشار إليه الإمام حجة الإسلام الغزالي وهو أنه لما بطل الجبر المحض بالضرورة وكون العبد خالقاً لأفعاله بالدليل، وجب الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد وهو أنها مقدورة بقدرة الله تعالى اختراعاً، وبقدرة العبد على وجه آخر من التعلق يعبر عندنا بالاكتساب وليس من ضرورة تعلق القدرة بالمقدور أن يكون على وجه الاختراع. إذ قدرة الله تعالى في الأزل متعلقة بالعالم من غير اختراع، ثم تتعلق به عند الاختراع نوعاً آخر من التعلق، فحركة العبد باعتبار نسبتها إلى قدرته تسمى كسباً له، وباعتبار نسبتها إلى قدرة الله تعالى خلقاً، فهي خلق للرب ووصف للعبد، وكسب له، وقدرته خلق للرب ووصف للعبد وليس بكسب له.
Something close to that is what is said: the origin of the movement is by the power of Allah and its specification is by the power of the servant, and it is acquisition, but it requires scrutiny. And it was said: the act which Allah the Exalted creates in the servant, and with it He creates a power for the servant related to it, is called acquisition for the servant, contrary to when that power is not created with it. And it was said: The servant has a power by which only relations and attributions differ, like determining one of the two sides of action and abandonment and preferring it, and no entity necessarily results from it. Rather, the relational matter which is required from the servant, and is not required when the effect exists, is acquisition. This is what they said: it is that by which the predestined occurs without the soundness of the agent's independence, and that which occurs in the locus of his power, unlike creation, for it is that by which the predestined occurs with the soundness of the agent's independence and that which does not occur in the locus of his power. Thus acquisition does not cause the existence of the predestined, but rather causes, inasmuch as it is acquisition, the attribution of the agent to that predestined. For this reason, it is the basis for differing relations, such as the act being obedience or disobedience, good or evil. For being described with evil by one's intention and will is evil, unlike the creation of evil, for it does not contradict wisdom and the praiseworthy outcome, but perhaps even includes them. The summary of the discourse is what Imam Hujjat al-Islam al-Ghazali pointed out: That since pure determinism was invalidated by necessity, and the servant being the creator of his actions by proof, moderation in belief became obligatory, which is that they are predestined by the power of Allah the Exalted by way of invention (ikhtira'), and by the power of the servant in another mode of relation which we call acquisition (al-iktisab). It is not necessary for the relation of power to the predestined to be in the mode of invention. The power of Allah the Exalted in pre-eternity was related to the world without invention, then it relates to it upon invention in another mode of relation. So the servant's movement, in terms of its relation to his power, is called his acquisition, and in terms of its relation to the power of Allah the Exalted, it is creation. Thus it is creation of the Lord and description of the servant, and acquisition for him. And his power is creation of the Lord and description of the servant, and not acquisition for him.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents three alternative definitions of kasb and concludes with al-Ghazali's summary. First, a view similar to al-Razi's but more specific: the origin of movement from Allah, its specification (ta'ayyun) from the servant, called kasb, but the author notes it requires scrutiny (فيه نظر). Second, another definition: the act created by Allah in the servant along with a power for the servant related to it is called kasb; if no such power is created, it is not kasb. Third, the more precise Ash'ari view: the servant has a power that only affects relations and preferences, not bringing about existence. The relational attribute that arises from the servant (like intention) is kasb. This view distinguishes kasb from khalq (creation): kasb occurs in the locus of the servant's power without the servant being independent, while creation occurs with the agent's independence and not in the locus of the creature's power. The passage then explains that kasb does not cause the existence of the act but rather causes the attribution of the act to the agent. This is why kasb is the basis for moral qualities: an act is evil only if the servant intends and wills it; the creation of evil by Allah is not evil because it includes wisdom. Finally, al-Ghazali's summary harmonizes the doctrines: pure determinism is false by necessity, and the human as creator is false by proof; the middle path is that actions are predestined by Allah's invention (ikhtira') and by the servant's power in a different mode called iktisab. He illustrates with Allah's eternal relation to the world: in pre-eternity, His power related to the world without invention (i.e., before creation), then at creation it relates differently. Similarly, the servant's movement is creation from Allah and acquisition from the servant.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think kasb implies that the human creates his own actions.
Correction: The text clarifies that kasb is a different mode of relation: the act is created by Allah and only attributed to the servant through acquisition; it does not involve creation by the human.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali's summary, what is the relationship between the servant's action and the power of Allah?
A) The action is created by Allah alone with no role for the servant
B) The action is created by the servant alone with no role for Allah
C) The action is created by Allah by invention and attributed to the servant by acquisition in a different mode
D) The action is a joint creation of both Allah and the servant

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's synthesis shows that actions are created by Allah (ikhtira') and acquired by the servant (iktisab) in a different mode of relation, preserving both divine power and human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly feel responsible for your actions when you know that Allah creates them? How does this lesson change your sense of accountability for a sin you committed today?
?
When you perform an act of obedience, do you attribute it to yourself or to Allah? According to this lesson, what should your attitude be? Are you willing to acknowledge both?
?
This lesson says that the act being good or evil is due to the servant's intention. What intention did you have for your last action? Will you examine your intentions more seriously now?

The First Proof Against Servant Power
The author refutes three logical possibilities for the act's occurrence and adds a further reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of the claim that the servant creates his own actions, based on the impossibility of the act occurring under joint or sole servant power.

Core Idea
The act cannot occur by the servant's power alone or jointly with God.

This lesson presents the first argument in the text against the doctrine that humans create their own actions. The author systematically examines every conceivable way an act could be attributed to the servant's power and shows each leads to an absurdity. By following this logical division, you will see why only God's power can be truly effective.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three 'إما' statements that divide the possibilities.
The author uses a logical division (taqsīm) to exhaust all possibilities.
2
Understand the first possibility
If the act occurs by the servant's power alone, then the weaker power would have preponderance over the stronger, which is absurd. Also, God would lose power over the act.
The additional argument about incapacitation reinforces this scenario.
3
Understand the second possibility
If the act occurs by both powers together, it would require two independent causes for the same effect, which is impossible.
This is a standard principle in kalam: two independent agents cannot both cause one effect.
4
Understand the third possibility
If the act occurs by neither power, then it would not occur at all, contradicting the assumption that it does occur. The non-occurrence without a cause is only due to an impediment, which leads to circular reasoning.
The author argues that the only possible impediment is occurrence by the other power, creating a contradiction.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how all possibilities lead to absurdity. Only God's power is truly effective, and its exercise confirms His power rather than diminishing it.
The final point about 'tqrīr' (confirmation) is key to the Ash'arite view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإما أن يقع بقدرة الغير وحده فيلزم ترجح أحد المتساويين، بل ترجح المرجوح لأن التقدير استقلال القدرتين مع أن قدرة الله تعالى أقوى. وإما أن يقع بكل من القدرتين، فيلزم اجتماع المستقلتين، وإما أن لا يقع بشيء منهما وهو أيضاً باطل، لأن التقدير وقوعه في الجملة ولأن التخلف عن المقتضى لا يكون إلا لمانع وما ذاك إلا الوقوع بالقدرة الثانية فلا ينتفي الوقوع بهما إلا إذا وقع بهما وهو محال، وأيضاً لو وقع بقدرة الغير لما بقي الله تعالى قدرة على إيجاده لاستحالة إيجاد الموجود، فيلزم كون العبد معجزاً للرب وهو محال بخلاف ما إذا أوجده الله تعالى بقدرته فإنه يكون تقريراً لقدرته لا تعجيزا.
Either it occurs by the power of another alone, so the preponderance of one of the two equals would be necessary, rather the preponderance of the inferior because the estimation is the independence of the two powers while the power of God Almighty is stronger. Or it occurs by both powers, so the meeting of two independents would be necessary. Or it does not occur by either of them, and that is also false, because it is estimated to occur in general, and because the non-occurrence of the effect without a cause is only due to an impediment, and that impediment is nothing but the occurrence by the second power, so the non-occurrence by both is not negated unless it occurs by both, which is impossible. Also, if it occurred by the power of another, God Almighty would no longer have power to bring it into existence because bringing an existing thing into existence is impossible, so it would necessitate that the servant is rendering the Lord incapable, which is impossible, unlike if God Almighty brings it into existence by His power, for that is a confirmation of His power, not an incapacitation.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against the claim that the servant creates his own actions. The argument is a logical division of possibilities. The author considers three scenarios for an act that is within both God's power and the servant's power: (1) The act occurs by the power of another alone (i.e., the servant), (2) by both powers together, or (3) by neither. Each scenario is refuted. The first leads to the preponderance of the weaker over the stronger, since God's power is stronger. The second requires two independent agents causing the same effect, which is impossible. The third fails because if neither causes the act, it would not occur at all, contradicting the assumption that it does occur. The author adds a further point: if the act occurred by the servant's power, God would lose power over it, implying the servant overpowers God—an absurdity. Thus, only God's power is effective, and its exercise confirms His power, not diminishes it.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about whether God or the servant has power over the act.
Correction: The argument is about the logical impossibility of the act occurring under joint or sole servant power, not about who has power per se.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Only God's power can effectively bring an act into existence; any attribution of independent creative power to the servant leads to logical absurdity.

?
The argument shows that attributing independent power to the servant leads to absurdity. Is there any area of your life where you act as if you are independent of God's power? What step can you take today to surrender that area to Him?

The Second Proof: Ignorance of Details Negates Creation
The author presents the second proof against human creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against human creation of actions, which shows that a creator must know all details of what he creates, and that humans are ignorant of the details of their actions.

Core Idea
A creator must know every detail of his creation.

This lesson presents a powerful argument: if we truly created our own actions, we would have to know every minute detail of them. Yet, as the examples of sleeping, walking, speaking, and writing show, we are often unaware of the intricate details of what we do. By the end, you'll see why this ignorance refutes the claim that we are the creators of our actions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional argument
The proof is a conditional: if we create our actions, we must know their details. Follow the logic carefully.
2
Examine the entailment (ملازمة)
Learn why the entailment holds: a specifier (مخصص) is needed to choose among possibilities, and that requires knowledge.
The author cites the Quranic verse to reinforce this.
3
Review the four examples
The author provides four cases—sleeper, walker, speaker, writer—to show we lack detailed knowledge. Study each example to see the pattern.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the consequent (that we know details) is false, the antecedent (that we create our actions) must be false. This disproves human creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الثاني الدليل الثاني أن العبد لو كان موجداً لأفعاله لكان عالماً بتفاصيلها واللازم باطل، أما الملازمة فلأن الإتيان بالأزيد والأنقص والمخالف ممكن فلا بد لرجحان ذلك النوع وذلك المقدار من مخصص هو القصد إليه، ولا يتصور ذلك إلا بعد العلم به، ولظهور هذه الملازمة يستنكر الخلق بدون العلم كقوله تعالى ألا يعلم من خلق) ويستدل بفاعلية العالم على عالمية الفاعل، وأما بطلان اللازم فلوجوه منها أن النائم تصدر عنه أفعال اختيارية لا شعور له بتفاصيل كمياتها وكيفياتها، ومنها أن الماشي إنساناً كان أو غيره يقطع مسافة معينة في زمان معين من غير شعور له بتفاصيل الأجزاء والأحياز التي بين المبدأ والمنتهى، ولا بالآنات التي منها يتألف ذلك الزمان ولا بالسكنات التي يتخللها تكون تلك الحركة ابطاء من حركة الفلك، أو بالحد الذي لها من وصف السرعة والبطؤ، ومنها أن الناطق يأتي بحروف مخصوصة على نظم مخصوص من غير شعور له بالأعضاء التي هي آلاتها، ولا بالهيئات والأوضاع التي تكون لتلك الأعضاء عند الإتيان بتلك الحروف، ومنها أن الكاتب يصور الحروف والكلمات بتحريك الأنامل من غير شعور له بما للأنامل من الأجزاء والأعضاء أعني العظام والغضاريف والأعصاب والعضلات والرباطات ولا بتفاصيل حركاتها وأوضاعها التي بها يتأتى تلك الصور والنقوش.
He said: The second proof: That if the servant were the creator of his actions, he would know their details, but the consequent is false. As for the entailment, because bringing about more, less, or different is possible, so there must be for the preponderance of that type and that amount a specifier, which is the intention towards it, and that is not conceivable except after knowledge of it. Due to the obviousness of this entailment, creation without knowledge is rejected, as His statement 'Does He who created not know?' and one infers from the agency of the knower the knowledge of the agent. As for the falsehood of the consequent, it is due to several aspects: among them that the sleeper performs voluntary actions without consciousness of the details of their quantities and qualities. Among them that the walker, whether human or other, traverses a certain distance in a certain time without consciousness of the details of the parts and spaces between the beginning and end, nor of the moments that compose that time, nor of the pauses in between that make that movement slower than the movement of the sphere, nor of the limit that it has in terms of speed and slowness. Among them that the speaker produces specific letters in a specific order without consciousness of the organs that are their instruments, nor of the states and positions of those organs when producing those letters. Among them that the writer forms letters and words by moving the fingers without consciousness of what the fingers have of parts and limbs, namely bones, cartilages, nerves, muscles, and ligaments, nor of the details of their movements and positions by which those forms and designs are achieved.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof. The argument is structured as a conditional: if the servant creates his actions, he must know all their details. The entailment is justified because an intentional act requires a specifier (مخصص) that determines its type and amount, and that specifier is the intention, which depends on knowledge. The author cites the Quranic verse 'Does He who created not know?' to reinforce that creation implies knowledge. The consequent (that the servant knows all details) is then proven false through four examples: the sleeper, the walker, the speaker, and the writer. In each case, the agent performs complex actions without awareness of the minute details—the sleeper does not know the exact quantities and qualities of his movements; the walker does not know the precise spatial and temporal breakdown of his steps; the speaker does not know the positioning of his speech organs; the writer does not know the detailed anatomy and movements of his fingers. Since we are clearly ignorant of these details, we cannot be the creators of our actions. Therefore, the original conditional’s consequent is false, so the antecedent (that the servant creates his actions) must also be false.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: We are fully conscious of all aspects of our actions.
Correction: The proof shows that in many actions (sleeping, walking, speaking, writing), we lack awareness of the detailed mechanics.

C) Reading, writing, thinking, and moving.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that since we are ignorant of the details of our actions, we cannot be their creators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If not, how can you claim to be the true creator of that walking? Will you now surrender your sense of self-authorship to the One who knows every detail?
?
Think of the last word you spoke. Did you consciously control the positions of your tongue, lips, and vocal cords? You didn't. Yet you produced it. Does this not show that your actions are beyond your full knowledge? How will this change your daily habit of attributing your speech to yourself rather than to Allah?
?
The sleeper acts without consciousness of his actions. You too are often 'asleep' to the details of your deeds. If you are unaware of so much, what right do you have to claim ownership of your actions? Will you today, in at least one action, pause and acknowledge that only Allah truly knows and creates it?

The Third Proof: Necessity of a Preponderator
Proof via preponderator necessity and reply to objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessity of a preponderator disproves human independent ability and how the objection regarding God's actions is answered by distinguishing eternal will from temporal causes.

Core Idea
A preponderator must exist for any choice; for God it is eternal, for the servant it leads to regress.

This proof tackles the heart of predestination by examining what genuine choice requires. If a servant could truly do or leave an action, when he does it something must tip the balance. That preponderator cannot come from him without infinite regress. An objection arises: doesn't God also choose? The answer reveals a key difference that preserves divine transcendence and undermines the claim of human independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the logical structure emerge naturally.
The passage ends abruptly; note the incomplete sentence.
2
Identify the conditional structure
Find the 'if-then' statement: 'If the action were by his power and choice, he would be capable of both doing and leaving.' Notice how the author sets up the consequent to be refuted.
3
Trace the argument about preponderator
Follow why the consequent is false: the act's occurrence over omission requires a preponderator that cannot come from the servant, leading to necessity and regress.
4
Understand the objection and answer
Read the objection that the same reasoning applies to God. Then see the answer: God's preponderator is His eternal will, while the servant's would be temporal and require another, nullifying independence.
5
Reflect on the cut-off
The passage ends with a repetition of the consequent. Consider what the author likely intended to complete: proving that the consequent leads to coercion, thus the conditional is false.
This is speculative based on the overall argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الثالث الدليل الثالث أنه لو كان فعل العبد بقدرته واختياره لكان متمكناً من فعله وتركه واللازم باطل لأنه لا بد من ترجح الفعل على الترك بلا مرجح لا يكون منه، ويجب عنده الفعل لامتناع الترجح بلا مرجح وتسلسل المرجحات ووجود الأثر بدون الوجوب. واعترض بأنه يرد على فعل الباري تعالى وبأن الوجوب بالاختيار لا ينافي الاستواء بحسب القدرة. وأجيب بأن المرجح ثمة أزلي هي الإرادة القديمة المتعلقة في الأزل بأن يوجد الفعل في وقته وههنا حادث يفتقر إلى مرجح آخر يبطل استقلال العبد وتمكنه من الترك) لو كان فعل العبد بقدرته واختياره لكان متمكناً من فعله وتركه، إذ لو لم يتمكن من الترك لزم الجبر وبطل الاختيار. لكن اللازم أعني التمكن من الفعل والترك
The third proof: That if the action of the servant were by his power and choice, he would be capable of doing it and leaving it, but the consequent is false because there must be a preponderance of the act over the omission without a preponderator that does not come from him, and the act becomes necessary for him due to the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator, and the regress of preponderators, and the existence of an effect without necessity. And it was objected that this applies to the act of God Almighty, and that necessity by choice does not contradict being equal in terms of power. And it was answered that the preponderator there is eternal, which is the eternal will related in eternity to the existence of the act in its time, while here it is temporal and requires another preponderator, which nullifies the independence of the servant and his ability to leave. If the action of the servant were by his power and choice, he would be capable of doing it and leaving it, for if he were not capable of leaving, coercion would follow and choice would be invalid. But the consequent, namely the capability of doing and leaving...
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof, which focuses on the concept of preponderance (ترجح). The author argues that if the servant truly had the ability (تمكن) to both do and leave an action, then when he does it, there must be a preponderator (مرجح) that tips the balance from omission to action. This preponderator cannot come from the servant himself because that would require another preponderator, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore, the act becomes necessary for the servant, which negates free will. The author then anticipates an objection: this argument seems to apply to God's actions as well, since God also chooses one action over another. The answer differentiates: for God, the preponderator is His eternal will, which is uncaused and does not negate His ability. For the servant, the preponderator would be a new, temporal factor, which undermines his independence and ability to refrain. The passage then reiterates the conditional: if the servant had power and choice, he would be capable of both doing and leaving; if he could not leave, that would be coercion (جبر) and negate choice. The text cuts off before completing the refutation of the consequent.

Misconception: The objection about God's actions completely refutes the proof, showing it applies to God as well.
Correction: The answer shows that God's eternal will is a self-sufficient preponderator, while the servant's would require a chain of causes, undermining the servant's independence.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of a preponderator for any contingent choice leads to either an infinite regress or a necessary cause; for God it is His eternal will, but for a creature it negates true independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Logical Argument Against Human Independence in Action
Refuting Mutazila by showing the need for an external preponderant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that refutes the Mutazila claim of human independence in action, and why the argument does not prove absolute determinism.

Core Idea
Every action's existence over non-existence requires a cause beyond the servant.

This passage presents a succinct dilemma that challenges the Mutazila doctrine of human independence. By analyzing whether the preponderance of action over inaction requires a preponderant, the argument systematically refutes the possibility of the servant being the ultimate source of his actions. However, the author carefully notes that this argument does not go so far as to prove that the servant creates nothing.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma
Notice the two branches: either action needs a preponderant or not. Understand each implication.
The dilemma is the core structure of the argument.
2
Trace the preponderant from the servant
Follow the reasoning: if the preponderant is from the servant, it leads to infinite regress or an external cause.
Infinite regress is impossible (محال).
3
Conclude the refutation
Understand why the servant cannot be independent, and note the limitation of the argument.
The argument targets the Mutazila position, not all positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باطل لأن رجحان الفعل على الترك، إما أن يتوقف على مرجح أو لا . فعلى الثاني يلزم رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن بلا مرجح ، وينسد باب إثبات الصانع ، ويكون وقوع الفعل بدلاً عن الترك محض الاتفاق من غير اختيار للعبد ، وعلى الأول : إن كان ذلك المرجح من العبد ينقل الكلام إلى صدوره عنه فيلزم التسلسل وهو محال، أو الانتهاء إلى مرجح لا يكون منه ، وإذا كان المرجح ابتداء أو بالآخرة لا من العبد بل من غيره، ثبت عدم استقلال العبد بالفعل، وعدم تمكنه من الترك، لأن الترك لم يجز وقوعه مع التساوي، فكيف مع المرجوحية، ولأن وجود الممكن ما لم ينته رجحانه إلى حد الوجوب لم يتحقق على ما مر ولا يخفى أن هذا إنما يفيد إلزام المعتزلة القائلين باستقلال العبد، واستناد الفعل إلى قدرته واختياره من غير جبر، ولا يفيد أن العبد ليس بموجد لأفعاله .
False, because the preponderance of action over inaction: either it depends on a preponderant or not. On the second, it would necessitate the preponderance of one of the two sides of a possible without a preponderant, and the door of establishing the Creator would be closed, and the occurrence of action instead of inaction would be pure coincidence without choice for the servant. And on the first: if that preponderant is from the servant, the discussion would transfer to its origination from him, leading to infinite regress, which is impossible; or to ending at a preponderant not from him. And if the preponderant is initially or ultimately not from the servant but from another, then the independence of the servant in action is not established, nor his ability to refrain, because inaction is not possible with equality, so how with inferiority? And because the existence of a possible, unless its preponderance reaches the level of necessity, does not occur, as has passed. And it is not hidden that this only benefits refuting the Mu'tazila who claim the independence of the servant and that action is based on his power and choice without compulsion, and it does not prove that the servant is not the creator of his actions.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument against the claim of human independence in actions, specifically refuting the Mutazila position. The argument begins by stating that the preponderance (رجحان) of action over inaction either requires a preponderant (مرجح) or not. If no preponderant is needed, then the occurrence of one possible over another is pure coincidence, which would close the door to proving the existence of the Creator (since that also requires a preponderant) and would deny the servant's choice. If a preponderant is required, and that preponderant comes from the servant, then the discussion shifts to how that preponderant originates from the servant, leading either to infinite regress (تسلسل) or to a preponderant not from the servant. If the preponderant is ultimately not from the servant, then the servant is not independent: he cannot refrain from the action because with equality it was impossible, and even more so with inferiority. The passage then notes that this argument only refutes the Mutazila who believe in complete independence, but it does not prove that the servant is not the creator of his actions (i.e., it does not establish absolute determinism).

Key Terms
رجحان
مرجح
تسلسل
استقلال
جبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that humans have no role in creating their actions (absolute determinism).
Correction: The author explicitly states the argument only refutes the Mutazila claim of complete independence and does not prove that the servant is not the creator of his actions.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the argument?
A) It proves that actions are completely determined by God.
B) It only refutes the Mutazila claim of independence, but does not prove the servant is not the creator of his actions.
C) It proves that the servant has no power at all.
D) It is a proof of free will.

Key Takeaway
The argument refutes the Mutazila claim of complete independence but does not negate the servant's role in creating actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author is careful to say this argument does not prove you are not the 'creator' of your actions. Right now, where do you place yourself on the line between total independence and total compulsion? If you truly believed your actions are not fully independent, what would change in how you pray, struggle against your desires, or rely on Allah?

First Two Mu'tazila Objections on Choice and God's Action
First two Mu'tazila objections and their responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Sunni theologians refute the Mu'tazila objections that the determinist argument contradicts necessary knowledge of human choice and that it would make God a necessitating cause.

Core Idea
Human choice is apparent, but ultimate determination is from God.

The Mu'tazila raise powerful objections against the determinist view, claiming it contradicts our direct experience of choice and would apply to God in a problematic way. This lesson presents the first two objections and the responses that distinguish between human and divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow of objections and responses. Then read the translation.
Notice the Quranic citations in the response to the first objection.
2
Identify the two objections
The first objection claims we know by necessity that we have choice. The second argues that the same reasoning would make God a necessitating cause. Find these in the text.
3
Reflect on the distinction
The responses distinguish between human will (temporal, dependent on God) and divine will (eternal, self-sufficient). Ask yourself: why does this matter for the overall debate?
The verifiers conclude that the outcome is compulsion (جبر).

وللمعتزلة ههنا اعتراضات أحدها أن ما ذكرتم استدلال في مقابلة الضرورة فلا يستحق الجواب، وذلك لأنا نعلم بالضرورة أن لنا مكنة واختياراً، وأنا إن شئنا الفعل فعلنا وإن شئنا الترك تركنا . وثانيها : أنه جار في فعل الباري فيلزم أن يكون موجباً لا مختاراً، وذلك لأن جميع ما لابد منه في إيجاد العالم إن كان حاصلاً في الأزل، لزم قدم العالم وصدوره عن الباري بطريق الوجوب من غير تمكن من الترك لامتناع التخلف عن تمام العلة، وإن لم يكن حاصلاً ينتقل الكلام إلى حدوث الأمر الذي لا بد منه ولا يتسلسل بل ينتهي إلى أمر أزلي يلزم معه المؤثر. ويعود المحذور. وثالثها : أن ترجيح المختار أحد المتساويين جائز كما في طريقي الهارب ، وقدحي العطّشنان لأن الإرادة صفة شأنها الترجيح والتخصيص من غير احتياج إلى مرجح، وإنما المحال الترجح بلا مرجح . ورابعها: أن المرجح الذي لا يكون من العبد هو تعلق الإرادة وخلوص الداعي، ووجوب الفعل معه لا ينافي الاختيار والتمكن من الفعل والترك بالنظر إلى القدرة . وأجيب عن الأول : بأن كلامنا في حصول المشيئة والداعية التي يجب معه الفعل أو الترك ولا خفاء في أنه ليس بمشيئتنا واختيارنا وإليه الإشارة بقوله تعالى وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله) وقوله قل كل من عند الله . ولهذا ذهب المحققون إلى أن المآل هو الجبر، وإن كان في الحال الاختيار، وأن الإنسان مضطر في صورة مختار. وعن الثاني : بأن للباري تعالى إرادة قديمة متعلقة في الأزل بأن يحدث الفعل في وقته، فلا يحتاج إلى مرجح آخر ليلزم التسلسل أو الانتهاء إلى ما ليس باختياره بخلاف إرادة العبد، فإنها حادثة يحدث تعلقها بالأفعال شيئاً فشيئاً، ويحتاج إلى دواعي مخصوصة متجددة من عند الله من غير اختيار للعبد فيها . وعن الثالث : بأنه إلزام على المعتزلة القائلين بوجوب المرجح في الفعل الاختياري لا القائلين بأنه يجوز للقادر ترجيح المساوي بل المرجوح، فإن الهارب يتمكن من سلوك أحد الطريقين، وإن كان مساوياً للآخر أو أصعب منه، وفيه نظر للقطع بأن ذلك لا يتصور إلا بداعية لا تكون بمشيئة العبد بل بمحض خلق الله تعالى. وحينئذ يجب الفعل، ولا يتمكن العبد من تركه ولا نعني بالانتهاء إلى الجبر والاضطرار هذا سوی و به يظهر الجواب عن الرابع .
The Mu'tazila have here objections: First, that what you have mentioned is an argument in the face of necessity, so it does not deserve a response; because we know necessarily that we have capability and choice, and that if we will the action we do it, and if we will to refrain we refrain. Second: that it applies to the act of the Creator, so it would necessitate that He be necessitating, not free; because all that is necessary for the creation of the world, if it was present in eternity, would necessitate the world's pre-eternity and its emanation from the Creator by way of necessity without ability to refrain, due to the impossibility of lagging behind the complete cause; and if it was not present, the discussion would shift to the temporality of that which is necessary, and there is no infinite regress but it ends in an eternal matter with which the effect is necessitated, and the same problem returns. Third: that the free agent's preponderance of one of two equals is permissible, as in the two paths of the fleeing person and the thirsty cups, because the will is an attribute whose function is preponderance and specification without needing a preponderant; the impossible is only preponderance without a preponderant. Fourth: that the preponderant which is not from the servant is the attachment of will and the pure motive, and the necessity of action with it does not contradict choice and the ability to act and refrain in relation to power. And it was answered: Regarding the first: that our discussion concerns the occurrence of volition and motive with which action or refraining is necessary, and it is not hidden that it is not by our volition and choice; and that is indicated by His statement, 'And you do not will unless God wills,' and His statement, 'Say: All is from God.' Therefore, the verifiers have concluded that the outcome is compulsion, even if there is choice in the present moment, and that man is compelled in the form of a free agent. Regarding the second: that the Creator, exalted, has an eternal will attached from eternity to cause the action at its time, so He does not need another preponderant that would necessitate infinite regress or ending in something not by His choice, unlike the servant's will, which is temporal, its attachment to actions occurring gradually, and it needs specific renewed motives from God without the servant having any choice in them. Regarding the third: that it is a refutation against the Mu'tazila who assert the necessity of a preponderant in voluntary action, not against those who say that the capable may preponderate the equal or even the inferior; for the fleeing person can take one of the two paths even if it is equal to the other or harder; but there is consideration because it is certain that that is not conceivable except with a motive that is not by the servant's volition but by the mere creation of God Almighty. Then the action becomes necessary, and the servant cannot abandon it; and we do not mean by ending in compulsion and constraint anything other than this; and by this the answer to the fourth appears.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents four objections from the Mu'tazila against the determinist argument and provides detailed responses. The first objection claims that the whole argument contradicts necessary knowledge—we know we have choice. The response explains that the discussion is about the volition and motive that make action or refraining necessary, and those are not from our volition, citing Quranic verses. The second objection argues that the same reasoning applied to God would make Him a necessitating cause, not free. The response distinguishes God's eternal will from the servant's temporal will; God's will is attached to actions from eternity without needing a further preponderant, avoiding infinite regress. The third objection states that a free agent can preponderate one of two equals without an external preponderant, using examples like the fleeing person. The response counters that this alleged possibility still depends on a motive created by God, so the action is necessary and not truly from the servant. The fourth objection claims that the preponderant is the attachment of will and pure motive, and necessity with it does not negate choice. The response is implied by the answer to the third, indicating that the will and motive are not from the servant's own choosing. Throughout, the responses emphasize that even if choice appears present, the ultimate determination is from God, leading to the conclusion that the outcome is compulsion (جبر).

ضرورة
مكنة
اختيار
إرادة
داعية
جبر
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the determinist argument ignores our obvious experience of free choice.
Correction: The response shows that apparent choice does not contradict ultimate determination; the volition and motive that lead to action are from God.

Checkpoint
What is the response to the Mu'tazila's second objection (that the argument applies to God)?
A) God is not subject to logic.
B) God's will is eternal and does not need a preponderant, unlike the servant's will.
C) The argument only applies to contingent beings.
D) The objection is invalid because God is free.

Key Takeaway
The first two objections are answered by distinguishing between human apparent choice and God’s ultimate determination, and between God’s eternal will and the servant’s temporal will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a strong desire to do something good or bad, do you pause to consider that even that motive is from God? How does this change your sense of accountability for your choices?
?
If ultimate compulsion from God is true, does that make you feel resigned or liberated? What is the difference between fatalism and trusting that God's will is wise?
?
Think of a decision you made recently that you were proud of. Can you honestly trace the initial inclination back to something you created, or did it feel like it was given to you? How does that affect your gratitude to God?

Divine Decree

Last Two Mu'tazila Objections on Preponderance and Motive
Last two Mu'tazila objections and their responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the determinist response addresses the Mu'tazila claim that a free agent can preponderate one equal without a preponderant and that the attachment of will and motive does not negate choice.

Core Idea
Even the appearance of preponderance depends on a motive created by God.

The Mu'tazila further argue that a free agent can choose between equals without an external preponderant, using the example of a fleeing person, and that the will's attachment does not contradict choice. This lesson presents these objections and the responses emphasizing that all motives are from God, making action necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation to understand the third and fourth objections and responses.
Note the examples: the fleeing person (طريقي الهارب) and the thirsty cups (قدحي العطّشان).
2
Analyze the third objection
The Mu'tazila claim that a free agent can preponderate one equal without a preponderant, like a fleeing person choosing a path. The response says this is only an argument against those who require a preponderant, but even this choice depends on a motive from God.
3
See the connection to the fourth objection
The fourth objection says the preponderant is the will's attachment and pure motive, and necessity does not negate choice. The response is implied: the motive is from God, so the outcome is compulsion.
The lesson ends with the conclusion that man is compelled in the form of a free agent.

Commentary

The response is implied by the answer to the third, indicating that the will and motive are not from the servant's own choosing. Divine Decree

The Fourth Proof: God's Knowledge Necessitates Action
A concise proof based on God's foreknowledge, with an objection and reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth proof in classical kalam uses God's foreknowledge to argue that human actions are necessary or impossible, thereby leaving no room for genuine human capability.

Core Idea
God's knowledge makes the future fixed; human capability is illusory.

This lesson examines a concise but powerful proof from the kalam tradition. The argument hinges on the premise that God's knowledge is infallible and all-encompassing. If God knows an action will occur, it cannot fail to occur, stripping the servant of any real power to do otherwise. As you read the passage, notice how the objection attempts to rescue human agency, but the reply reinforces the determinist conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses very concise, almost syllogistic language.
2
Identify the core logical move
The proof presents a dichotomy: either God knows the action will occur (then it is necessary) or He knows it will not occur (then it is impossible). This move eliminates any middle ground.
3
Consider the objection and response
The objector claims that what God knows occurs through the servant's power and choice. The reply: even then, the knowledge necessitates it, so the same determinism remains. Also, this would contradict the act of the Creator.
The phrase 'contradicted by the act of the Creator' is a brief hint that a similar argument would apply to God's own actions.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Ask yourself: if this proof is sound, does it leave any room for human free will in the sense of genuine contingency? The conclusion that the action 'does not remain within the capability of the servant' is stark.
This connects to the larger debate in kalam about kasb (acquisition) and divine determinism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الرابع) الدليل الرابع معلوم الله تعالى من فعل العبد . أما وقوعه فيجب أو لا وقوعه فيمتنع، فلا يبقى في مكنة العبد، وإن كان ممكناً في نفسه . فإن قيل : المعلوم وقوعه بقدرة العبد واختياره . قلنا : فيجب ذلك ويعود المحذور ونوقض بفعل الباري).
He said: The fourth proof: What is known by God Almighty concerning the action of the servant: if its occurrence is known, it is necessary; if not, it is impossible. Thus it does not remain within the capability of the servant, even if it is possible in itself. If it is said: What is known to occur is by the servant's power and choice, we say: then that (knowledge) necessitates it, and the same problem returns; and it is contradicted by the act of the Creator.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents a concise proof (الدليل الرابع) based on God's knowledge (معلوم الله). The argument: God knows from eternity whether a particular action of the servant will occur or not. If He knows it will occur, then it must occur (يجب); if He knows it will not occur, then it is impossible (يمتنع). Consequently, the action is not within the capability (مكنة) of the servant, even if it is possible in itself (i.e., considered apart from God's knowledge). An objection is raised: What God knows to occur happens through the servant's power and choice (بقدرة العبد واختياره). The reply: Even then, the knowledge necessitates the occurrence, so the same problem (that the action is determined) remains. Additionally, this is contradicted by the act of the Creator (فعل الباري) — presumably meaning that if God's knowledge makes the action necessary, then the servant's power is not truly effective. The proof is a standard kalam argument that God's foreknowledge is incompatible with free will in the sense of genuine contingency.

Key Terms
معلوم
علم الله
وجوب
امتناع
مكنة
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's knowledge is neutral or does not impose necessity, leaving the action genuinely contingent.
Correction: The argument shows that if God's knowledge is infallible, the known must occur exactly as known, thus the action is necessary or impossible, not contingent.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the fourth proof?
A) God's knowledge is unlimited.
B) If God knows an action will occur, it must occur, leaving no room for the servant's capability.
C) The servant has free will regardless of God's knowledge.
D) The proof is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof uses God's foreknowledge to eliminate genuine human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge makes every action of yours either necessary or impossible, what happens to your sense of personal responsibility? Can you still feel genuine guilt for a sin you committed, or gratitude for a good deed, knowing that it was predetermined?
?
Think of a time you strongly felt you made a free choice. How does this proof challenge that memory? Are you willing to accept that your choice was not really within your capability?
?
The proof ends by saying the same logic would contradict the act of the Creator. Does that give you any relief, or does it deepen the tension? What does it mean that even God's actions are not subject to external necessity? How does that affect your trust in His justice?

Divine Decree

Answer to the First Objection on Divine Will
Answering the first objection that human will and motive are from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theologians answer the objection that human will and motive come from God, and how they reconcile divine predestination with apparent human choice.

Core Idea
Human actions are compelled by God's will, yet we experience choice — we are 'compelled in the form of one who chooses.'

This passage tackles a common objection to the Ash'ari view of divine will. Critics argue that if God creates both the will and the motive to act, then human beings have no real agency. The Ash'ari response does not deny this logic but instead redefines the relationship between divine causation and human experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal English translation. Notice the flow: an answer, a clarification, scriptural evidence, and a conclusion.
2
Identify the core claim
The answer states that the discussion concerns the occurrence of will (al-mashi'a) and motive (al-da'iya) that necessitate action or omission. Understand that these are not under human control — they come from God.
This clarifies that the objection is about divine causation, not human freedom in the ordinary sense.
3
Examine the scriptural proofs
Look at the two Quranic verses cited: 'But you do not will except that Allah wills' and 'Say: All is from Allah'. How do these support the argument that will and motive originate from God?
The verses are used to show that even our willing is dependent on God's will.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The verifiers (al-muhaqqiqun) conclude that the ultimate outcome (al-ma'al) is compulsion (jabr), yet in the present moment there is choice (ikhtiyar). Reflect on the phrase 'the human is compelled in the form of one who chooses' (mudtarr fi surat mukhtar).
This is the key reconciliation: objectively actions are determined, but subjectively we experience freedom.
5
Connect to broader theology
Consider how this answer fits into the Ash'ari position on qadar (divine decree). Does it solve the objection? What tensions remain?
This passage is a classic articulation of Ash'ari occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب عن الأول : بأن كلامنا في حصول المشيئة والداعية التي يجب معه الفعل أو الترك ولا خفاء في أنه ليس بمشيئتنا واختيارنا وإليه الإشارة بقوله تعالى وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله) (۱) وقوله قل كل من عند الله (٢) . ولهذا ذهب المحققون إلى أن المآل هو الجبر، وإن كان في الحال الاختيار، وأن الإنسان مضطر في صورة مختار.
And it was answered to the first: that our discussion concerns the occurrence of will and motive with which the action or omission is necessarily connected, and there is no concealment that it is not by our will and choice. And to this is the indication by His statement: 'But you do not will except that Allah wills' (1) and His statement: 'Say: All is from Allah' (2). And for this the verifiers went to the position that the final outcome is compulsion, even if in the present there is choice, and that the human is compelled in the form of one who chooses.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage answers the first objection against the Ash'ari position that human actions are ultimately from God's will. The answer clarifies that the discussion concerns the occurrence of the will and motive that necessitate action or omission, and it is clear that these are not under human control. The objection likely assumed that if will and motive come from God, then humans have no choice. The answer affirms that the ultimate outcome (al-ma'al) is indeed compulsion (jabr), yet in the present moment there is a sense of choice (ikhtiyar). The human is described as 'compelled in the form of one who chooses' (mudtarr fi surat mukhtar). This is a classic Ash'ari formulation balancing divine determinism with apparent human agency.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'aris deny all human choice; this passage shows they affirm a subjective experience of choice within an ultimately predetermined framework.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that in the present there is choice (ikhtiyar), so Ash'aris do not deny the human experience of choosing, even if the ultimate determination is from God.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response to the first objection affirms that ultimately human actions are compelled by God, yet in our experience we have choice; we are 'compelled in the form of one who chooses.'

?

The Eternal Will: Why God's Will Needs No New Motives
Distinguishing eternal divine will from temporal human will to refute infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between God's eternal will and human temporal will, and why this distinction refutes the objection of infinite regress.

Core Idea
God's eternal will needs no fresh motives; human will depends on them.

This passage addresses a key objection against divine predestination: if God wills all actions, wouldn't that require an endless chain of motives? The answer lies in the fundamental difference between the eternal, self-sufficient will of the Creator and the temporal, dependent will of the creature. Let's see how this distinction dissolves the problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then review the literal translation. Notice the contrast between divine will (إرادة قديمة) and human will (إرادة العبد).
Pay attention to key terms like 'preponderator' (مرجح) and 'infinite regress' (التسلسل).
2
Contrast the two wills
Identify how the passage describes God's will versus the servant's will. God's will is eternal and attached in pre-eternity; the servant's will is temporal and depends on renewed motives.
The contrast is the core of the argument.
3
Understand why the objection fails
Reflect on why the objection of infinite regress does not apply: God's will requires no new preponderator at each moment, unlike human will.
This is the ultimate takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الثاني : بأن للباري تعالى إرادة قديمة متعلقة في الأزل بأن يحدث الفعل في وقته، فلا يحتاج إلى مرجح آخر ليلزم التسلسل أو الانتهاء إلى ما ليس باختياره بخلاف إرادة العبد، فإنها حادثة يحدث تعلقها بالأفعال شيئاً فشيئاً، ويحتاج إلى دواعي مخصوصة متجددة من عند الله من غير اختيار للعبد فيها .
And about the second: that for the Exalted Creator there is an eternal will, attached in pre-eternity to bring about the action at its time, so it does not need another preponderator to necessitate an infinite regress or ending in what is not by His choice, unlike the will of the servant, for it is temporal, its attachment to actions occurs gradually, and it needs specific renewed motives from Allah without any choice of the servant in them.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage answers the second objection, which likely claimed that if human actions are determined by God's will, then an infinite regress of motives would arise, or something outside God's control. The answer distinguishes between God's eternal will (irada qadima) and the servant's temporal will. God's will is eternal and attached in pre-eternity to the action occurring at its specific time, so it requires no additional preponderator (murajjih) at each moment. In contrast, the servant's will is temporal; its attachment to actions occurs gradually (shay'an fa shay'an) and depends on specific renewed motives from Allah, over which the servant has no choice. Thus, the objection conflates the two types of will and fails.

Key Terms
إرادة قديمة
مرجح
التسلسل
إرادة العبد
دواعي مخصوصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's will also requires external motives to determine each action, leading to an infinite regress of motives.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God's will is eternal and attached in pre-eternity to the action at its time, so it does not need a new preponderator. It is self-sufficient, unlike human will which depends on renewed motives.

Checkpoint

B) Because it is attached in pre-eternity to the action at its time
C) Because God is ignorant of future
D) Because human will is eternal

Key Takeaway
God's eternal will is self-sufficient and attached in pre-eternity to each action, whereas human will is temporal and depends on renewed motives from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Knowing that Allah's will is complete and needs no further input, why do you still worry about the future? What does your anxiety reveal about your belief in His eternal decree, and what step will you take today to entrust that worry to Him?

Answering the Objection of Equal Alternatives
Refuting the objection of equal alternatives by showing even they require a divine motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response to the objection of equal alternatives argues that even such choices require a divinely created motive, affirming determinism.

Core Idea
Even equal alternatives require a motive from Allah.

This passage addresses a key objection to determinism: that humans can choose between equally good options, implying free will. The response shows that the Mu'tazilite position requiring a preponderator is not applicable, and even in equal cases, a divine motive is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'هذا سوی' might be unclear; interpret in context.
2
Identify the two groups mentioned
The passage distinguishes between Mu'tazilites who require a preponderator, and those who allow preferring equal or inferior. Understand the difference.
3
Understand the key argument about motive
The passage argues that even in the case of equal alternatives, a motive (da'iya) is needed, and that motive is created by Allah, not the servant. This leads to necessity.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the action becomes necessary and the servant cannot leave it. How does this answer the objection?
This connects to the overall determinist stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الثالث : بأنه إلزام على المعتزلة القائلين بوجوب المرجح في الفعل الاختياري لا القائلين بأنه يجوز للقادر ترجيح المساوي بل المرجوح، فإن الهارب يتمكن من سلوك أحد الطريقين، وإن كان مساوياً للآخر أو أصعب منه، وفيه نظر للقطع بأن ذلك لا يتصور إلا بداعية لا تكون بمشيئة العبد بل بمحض خلق الله تعالى. وحينئذ يجب الفعل، ولا يتمكن العبد من تركه ولا نعني بالانتهاء إلى الجبر والاضطرار هذا سوی و به يظهر الجواب عن الرابع .
And about the third: that it is an imposition on the Mu'tazilites who affirm the necessity of a preponderator in voluntary action, not on those who say that it is permissible for the capable one to prefer the equal or even the inferior, for the fleeing person is able to take one of two paths even if it is equal to the other or more difficult. And there is a consideration in it, because it is certain that that is not conceivable except by a motive that is not by the servant's will but by pure creation of Allah the Exalted. And then the action becomes necessary, and the servant is not able to leave it. And we do not mean by the conclusion to compulsion and coercion this [؟] and with it the answer to the fourth appears.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage answers the third objection, which likely argued that a person can choose between two equal alternatives (e.g., two equally good roads for escape), demonstrating freedom. The answer says this objection is an imposition (ilzam) specifically on the Mu'tazilites who require a preponderator for voluntary action, not on those who allow preferring an equal or even inferior option. Even in the case of equal alternatives, a motive (da'iya) is needed, and that motive is not by the servant's will but by pure creation of Allah. Consequently, the action becomes necessary, and the servant cannot leave it. The final sentence is unclear but indicates that this leads to the answer to the fourth objection.

Correction: The passage shows that even equal alternatives require a divine motive, but the action still occurs through the servant's power, albeit necessitated by the motive.

The Fourth Proof: Divine Knowledge Necessitates Action
Core proof and initial objections and answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core argument of the fourth proof: that God's knowledge of an action's occurrence or non-occurrence makes it necessary or impossible, leaving no real power in the servant.

Core Idea
What God knows must happen cannot be avoided.

This proof is a key argument in the debate about human free will and divine predestination. It begins with a simple premise: if God knows an action will occur, it must occur; if He knows it will not, it cannot. This seems to eliminate any genuine choice for the servant. The passage presents this proof and then responds to the objection that the action might still be within the servant's power.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Begin by reading the opening lines where the proof is stated. Notice the key terms: 'must occur' (يجب) and 'impossible' (يمتنع).
The proof rests on the idea that divine knowledge is infallible.
2
Examine the first objection
Consider the objection that what God knows may occur by the servant's power and choice. How does the proof handle this?
The answer insists that even if the action is by the servant's power, it still must occur, leading to compulsion.
3
Understand the distinction between ilzam and itmam
The author notes that some proofs aim only for 'ilzam' (forcing an admission) rather than 'itmam' (complete proof). This is a methodological point.
This helps you see the limits of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الرابع) الدليل الرابع : معلوم الله تعالى من فعل العبد . أما وقوعه فيجب أو لا وقوعه فيمتنع، فلا يبقى في مكنة العبد، وإن كان ممكناً في نفسه . فإن قيل : المعلوم وقوعه بقدرة العبد واختياره . قلنا : فيجب ذلك ويعود المحذور ونوقض بفعل الباري). قد ثبت أن الله تعالى عالم بالجزئيات (۱) ما كان وما سيكون، وأنه يستحيل عليه الجهل وكل ما علم الله أنه يقع يجب وقوعه، وكل ما علم الله أنه لا يقع يمتنع وقوعه، نظراً إلى تعلق العلم، وإن كان ممكناً في نفسه وبالنظر إلى ذاته، ولا شيء من الواجب والممتنع باقياً في مكنة العبد بمعنى أنه إن شاء فعله وإن شاء تركه. فإن قيل : يجوز أن يعلم الله تعالى أن فعل العبد يقع بقدرته واختياره، فلا يكون خارجاً عن مكنته . من قلنا : فيجب أن يقع البتة بقدرته واختياره بحيث لا يتمكن من اختيار الترك، وهذا هو المراد بالانتهاء إلى الاضطرار. غاية الأمر أن يكون بإيجاده لكن لا على وجه الاستقلال والاختيار التام كما هو مذهب المعتزلة. وقد أشرنا إلى أن القصد (٢) بعض الأدلة إلى الالزام دون الإتمام نعم يرد نقض الدليل بفعل الباري تعالى الجريانه فيه مع الاتفاق على كونه بقدرته واختياره ويمكن دفعه بأن الاختياري ما يكون الفاعل متمكناً من تركه عند إرادة فعله لا بعده، وهذا متحقق في فعل الباري، لأن إرادته قديمة متعلقة في الأزل بأنه يقع في وقته وجائز أن يتعلق حينئذ بتركه (۳) ، وليس حينئذ سابقة علم ليتحقق الوجوب، أو الامتناع إذ لا قبل للأزل، فالحاصل أن تعلق العلم والإرادة معاً فلا محذور بخلاف إرادة العبد، وتقرير الإمام في المطالب أنه لما وجب في الأزل وقوع الفعل أو لا وقوعه في وقته لزم أن يكون لهذا الوجوب سبب ، وليس من العبد لأن الحادث لا يصلح سبباً للأزلي . بل من الله تعالى . وليس هو العلم لأنه تابع للمعلوم، لا مستتبع بل القدرة والإرادة، إذ بهما التأثير فثبت أن المؤثر في فعل العبد قدرة الله تعالى إما ابتداء أو بوسط وهو المطلوب، وهذا ضعيف جداً، لكن النقض مندفع عنه. العالية (٤) هو
He said (the fourth): The fourth proof: What God knows of the servant's action: if its occurrence [is known], it is necessary; or its non-occurrence [is known], it is impossible. So nothing remains in the servant's ability, even if it is possible in itself. If it is said: The thing known to occur by the servant's power and choice. We say: Then that is necessary, and the same problem returns. And it is objected by the action of the Creator. It has been established that God the Exalted knows particulars (1), what was and what will be, and that ignorance is impossible for Him. And everything that God knows will occur must occur, and everything that God knows will not occur cannot occur, from the perspective of the attachment of knowledge, even if it is possible in itself and from the perspective of its essence. And nothing of the necessary and the impossible remains in the servant's ability in the sense that if he wills he does it and if he wills he leaves it. If it is said: It is permissible that God the Exalted knows that the servant's action will occur by his power and choice, so it is not outside his ability. From [that] we say: Then it must necessarily occur by his power and choice such that he is not able to choose leaving. And this is what is meant by the conclusion to compulsion. The only difference is that it is by His creation but not in the manner of independence and complete choice as is the doctrine of the Mu'tazilites. And we have indicated that the intent of some proofs is imposition (ilzam) not completion (itmam). Yes, a counterexample to the proof arises from the action of the Creator the Exalted, because it applies to it [the same reasoning] despite agreement that it is by His power and choice. And it can be repelled by [saying] that the voluntary is that of which the agent is able to leave at the time of willing the action, not after it. And this is realized in the action of the Creator, because His will is eternal, attached in pre-eternity that it will occur at its time, and it is possible that it be attached then to its leaving (3), and there is not then prior knowledge to necessitate necessity or impossibility, since there is no before for pre-eternity. So the result is that the attachment of knowledge and will together, so there is no problem, unlike the will of the servant. And the exposition of the Imam in al-Matalib [al-'Aliya] that when the occurrence or non-occurrence of the action at its time became necessary in pre-eternity, it follows that there is a cause for this necessity, and it is not from the servant because the temporal cannot be a cause for the eternal. Rather from God the Exalted. And it is not knowledge because it follows the known, not a necessitator, but rather power and will, for effect is by them. So it is established that the influencer in the servant's action is the power of God the Exalted, either directly or indirectly, and that is what is sought. And this is very weak, but the counterexample is repelled from it. al-ʿĀliya (4) it is.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof and its extensive defense. The proof argues that whatever God knows about a servant's action—whether it will occur or not—is necessary or impossible respectively, leaving no real power in the servant. An objection states that God may know the action will occur by the servant's power and choice, but the answer insists this still necessitates the action, leading to compulsion. A counterexample is raised: the same reasoning would apply to God's own actions, implying they also are compelled. The answer distinguishes that a voluntary act is one the agent can leave at the moment of willing, not after; God's will is eternal and could have been attached to leaving, so no compulsion. The passage then references al-Razi's argument in al-Matalib al-ʿAliya, which posits that the eternal necessity of occurrence requires a cause from God alone—power and will—not from the servant. However, the author notes this is very weak (da'if jiddan), though the counterexample is deflected.

Key Terms
معلوم الله
مكنة العبد
الوجوب
الامتناع
تعلق العلم
الاضطرار
الالزام
الإتمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The action is possible but not certain
B) The action must occur
C) The action is impossible
D) The action is left to the servant's choice

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof argues that God's knowledge necessitates the occurrence or non‑occurrence of actions, making genuine human choice impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you willing to accept that your 'choices' are in fact necessary? What would that mean for your relationship with God and your daily striving?
?
The proof reduces human action to necessity under divine knowledge. Does this thought make you feel liberated from guilt or trapped in fatalism? What would it cost you to truly believe that you have no ultimate choice?
?
The author distinguishes between a proof meant to force an admission (ilzam) and a complete proof (itmam). When you defend your own beliefs, are you content with merely winning an argument, or do you seek a complete understanding that satisfies your soul? How often do you impose arguments on yourself without fully embracing their implications, and what would change if you didn't?
